3 
INTRODUCTION 

The  relevance  of 

this  work  stems  from  geopolitical,  economic,  and 

environmental  dimensions  that  have  shaped  global  energy  dynamics  in recent years. 

The transformation of global energy markets over the past two decades is of particular 

importance  in  the  context  of  current  tensions  between  Russia,  the  European  Union, 

and  the  United  States.  This  research  explores  how  the  United  States  —  through  its 

shale  revolution,  growing  liquefied  natural  gas  (LNG)  exports,  sanctions  policy, 

energy  diplomacy  and  other  implicit  and  explicit  tools  —  has  altered  global  supply 

structures and reshaped strategic alignments and dependencies. Examining the impact 

of these shifts on Russia's complex relationship with the EU, this study demonstrates 

the 

intersection  of  energy  security,  great  power 

rivalries  and  domestic 

decision-making  dynamics  of  states.  Understanding  the  U.S.  strategies  and  their 

influence on the EU may bring valuable insights to Russian policymakers and shed a 

light  on  the  future  of  global  energy  governance  in  the  developing  multipolar  world. 

The  European  Union  (EU)  has  been  an important Russian energy partner for several 

decades,  while  also  being  a  close partner of the United States. Given the historically 

rival  nature  of  Russia-U.S.  relations,  the  U.S.  had  intentions  to  prevent  close 

EU-Russia  cooperation  and  integration  (except  for  a  short  friendly  period  in  1990s, 

which was also not characterised by high support of new Russian state developments). 

Therefore, the factor of U.S. influence has always been present in EU-Russia relations 

and  is  of  a  particular  interest  given  the  current  Russia-West  hybrid  war  that  began 

after  the  launch  of  the  Special  Military  Operation  in  2022  (Russian  Ministry  of 

Foreign  Affairs,  2023).  It  is  very  important  for  Russia  to  understand  the  European 

Union’s  independent  motivations  in  combination  with  the  U.S.  factor,  to  be  able  to 

develop a substantial foreign policy, especially regarding energy. Nowadays, the U.S. 

also  tries  to  influence  post-Soviet  states,  which  are  under  strong  interest  of  Russia. 

These  developments  support  the  relevance  of  understanding  U.S.  tools  and  methods 

 
of influence on Russian partners.  

4 

The  aim  of  this  study  is  to  analyze  the  instruments  of  the  U.S.  influence  on 

global  energy  markets  through capturing the combination of tools, including implicit 

ones, that are not openly promoted, and to assess their influence on energy relations of 

the EU and Russia.  

The  objectives  of  the  research  include  assessing  the  U.S.’s use of key foreign 

policy  instruments  in  shaping  the  global  energy  landscape;  exploring  historical 

context  of  energy  in  international  relations,  including  the  U.S.’s  early  energy 

influence;  follow  and  assess  the  evolution  of  U.S.  energy  policy  in  2008-2024, 

particularly the formation of strategies designed to secure energy dominance; examine 

the  strategic  shifts  in  U.S.  energy  policy,  especially  in  response  to  key  geopolitical 

events and market changes, such as the shale revolution, the rise of LNG exports, and 

the  U.S.’s  increasing role in European energy security. Ultimately, the research seeks 

to  evaluate  how  these  U.S.  actions  have  altered  global  energy  dynamics,  with  a 

particular  emphasis  on  the  reduction  of  Russia’s  influence  in  European  energy 

markets. 

The  object  of  this  work  is the US energy policy and according transformation 

of the world energy markets, while the subject is the impact of the U.S. energy policy 

on the EU-Russia energy relations.  

The research question is the following: How did the U.S. impact world energy 

markets  in  2008-2024,  and  how  did  this  transformation  and  the US policy influence 

Russia  and  EU-Russia  relations  in  the given period? This question allows to address 

the  research  gap  while  being  directed  on  discovering  actually  useful knowledge and 

relevant  insights  into  the  intersection  of  energy  markets  and  geopolitics,  shedding 

light on critical shifts in international relations. 

It  is  important  to  overview  and  assess  the  existing  knowledge  on  the topic by 

conducting  a  literature  review.  To  begin,  focus  is  on  the U.S. role in global energy 

markets  and  shale  revolution.  Daniel  Yergin  in  ‘The  new  map:  Energy,  climate, and 

 
5 
the clash of nations’ (2020) offers an account of how the U.S. shale revolution, LNG 

export  surge,  and  domestic  energy  policy  shifts  have  reconfigured  global  energy 

flows.  He  claims  that  energy  self-sufficiency  underpinned  American  strategic 

confidence,  even  as  Biden-era  policies  created  tension  between  climate  goals  and 

fossil fuel dominance. Yergin also argues that Russia’s energy leverage in Europe has 

been  undermined  by  diversification  and  sanctions  but  this  was  a  point  of  internal 

growth  and  new  partnerships.  Yergin  also  provides  a  good  historical  account  of 

development  of  energy  assets  as  political  ones  in  ‘The  prize:  The  epic  quest for oil, 

money,  and  power’  (1991).  Aguilera  and  Radetzki  (2014) provide a macroeconomic 

and  geopolitical  account  of  the  U.S.  shale  revolution’s  transformative  impact  on 

global energy markets. They emphasize that technological breakthroughs allowed the 

U.S.  to  dramatically  increase natural gas and oil production between 2005 and 2013, 

overtaking  Russia  as the world’s largest gas producer by 2009. The article links U.S. 

shale  expansion  to  shifting  global  gas  trade  patterns,  the  emergence  of  LNG  export 

capacity, and downward pressure on gas prices, which has weakened Russia’s pricing 

power in Europe: Gazprom was forced to renegotiate long-term contracts due to more 

competitive spot pricing catalyzed by redirected U.S. LNG. Blackwill and O’Sullivan 

(2014)  also  argue  that  the  shale  revolution  enabled  the  USA  to  weaken  Russia  as  a 

producer  by  helping  European  allies  negotiate  better  terms  with  Gazprom.  The 

authors  highlight  that  Washington  began  using energy policy strategically — linking 

LNG export approvals to foreign policy objectives and launching diplomatic tools like 

the  Energy  Governance  and  Capacity  Initiative.  O’Sullivan (2017) points to positive 

consequences  of  ‘the  new  energy  abundance’  —  as  a  possibility  for  the  USA  and 

China  to  decrease  conflict  and  cooperate  on  energy  terms.  Andreas  (2015) 

demonstrates  that  the  shale  revolution  gave  the  USA  enormous  advantage  and 

changed global energy competition. Sargsyan (2022) shows how U.S. external energy 

policy  operationalizes  energy  security through diversified diplomacy. He emphasizes 

the  spatial  dimension  of  U.S.  energy  engagement,  with  different  instruments  across 

 
6 
developed  (e.g.,  EU,  Japan) and resource-rich developing regions (e.g., Africa, Latin 

America).  Particularly  relevant  is  his  classification  of  direct  tools  (LNG  exports, 

sanctions,  technology  transfer)  and  indirect  tools  (legislative  assistance,  governance 

support, energy diplomacy). Davtyan (2018) analyzes how the U.S. pursuit of energy 

dominance  under  Trump  aimed  to  displace  Russian  influence,  particularly  via  LNG 

exports  and  sanctions.  However,  he  argues  that  high  costs,  infrastructure  gaps,  and 

market  realities  limited  U.S.  ambitions,  especially  in  Europe  where  Russian  gas 

remained  competitive.  Khlopov  (2019)  analyzed  the  internal  contradictions  in  the 

Trump administration’s energy dominance strategy, highlighting how expanded fossil 

fuel  production  was  prioritized  as  a  tool  of  foreign policy and national security. The 

article provides a useful contrast between Trump’s geopolitical use of shale and LNG 

exports  and  domestic  resistance  from  environmental  and  progressive  forces 

advocating  for  the  Green  New  Deal.  While  acknowledging  the  geopolitical  gains  of 

becoming  a  net exporter, Khlopov underscores that long-term U.S. energy leadership 

may  depend  more  on  innovation  in  renewables  than  fossil  fuels.  However,  many  of 

the  existing  books  and  articles lost their relevance due to new geopolitical shifts and 

subsequent  energy  market  dynamics.  There  is  a  need  for  more  modern  research  to 

account for these new factors.  

Then  there  is  literature  on  the  history  of  energy  relations  between  Russia  and 

the  EU.  Stern  (2006)  provides  a  good  historical  background  of  energy conditions in 

Europe  and  claims  that  energy  cooperation  between  Russia and the European Union 

was  rising  for  decades,  leading  to  the  increase  of  interdependence,  and  mutually 

beneficial  trade  conditions.  Gustafson  (2020)  presents  a  detailed  historical  and 

institutional  analysis  of  the  ‘gas  bridge’  between  Russia  and  Europe,  tracing  its 

evolution from Cold War cooperation to a fragile and politicized structure in the 21st 

century.  He  shows  how  natural  gas  trade  stabilized  East–West  relations  through 

long-term  contracts,  but  also  how the EU’s liberalization and decarbonization efforts 

have  weakened  this  system.  While  Gazprom  acted  largely  commercially  in  Western 

 
7 
Europe,  Gustafson  argues  that  in  Central  and  Eastern  Europe,  gas  was  wielded  as  a 

geopolitical  instrument.  The  breakdown  of  mutual  trust  and  the  EU’s  shift  toward 

market-based  governance  now  threaten  the  bridge’s  stability,  making  its dismantling 

both  geopolitically  risky  and  structurally  difficult.  Boris Barkanov has examined the 

geo-economic  strategies  employed  by  Russia,  particularly  through  Gazprom,  to 

maintain  its  influence  over  European  energy  markets,  showing  that  integration  was 

mutually  desirable  (Barkanov,  2018).  However,  cooperative  incentives  have  slowly 

declined  in the EU. Gas crises pushed the transition to renewable energy and general 

EU  concerns  about  Russia.  Siddi  (2022)  explores  several  Ukrainian  crises  and  their 

influence  on  making  EU-Russia  relations  more  sensitive.  The  author  also  assesses 

energy  cooperation  as  beneficial  for  both  parties,  but  constantly  undermined  by 

political concerns.  

Finally,  the  literature  on  the  U.S.-EU  relations  will  be  useful.  Smith  (2021) 

shows the dependent position of the EU under the USA. He identifies it as a subaltern 

role of the EU where it acts as a channel for U.S. policies and preferences, particularly 

in areas like security and economic organization. This suggests a level of dependence 

on  U.S.  decisions.  Smith  and  Steffenson  (2023)  in  ‘The  EU  and  the  USA: 

Competitive Cooperation in. International Relations and the European Union’ suggest 

deep historical ties in the Transatlantic community which stems from the beginning of 

European  cooperation,  described  as  US-driven  with  aims  of  contrasting  the  Soviet 

communist  bloc.  In  modern  context  such  basis  for historical ties may be reflected in 

current  actions  of 

these  actors.  Moreover,  Kirshner  (2014)  shows  major 

interconnectedness  of  the  United  States  and  the  European  Union,  illustrating 

implications  of  economic  distress  in  Europe  on  U.S.  financial  stability  and  strategic 

interests, so the economy of the European Union has always been crucially important 

for  the  U.S.  Despite  a  strong  foundation  of  shared  values  and  institutional 

cooperation,  Cuciurianu  (2014)  emphasizes  that  the  U.S.-EU  relationship  remains 

defined  by  ‘competitive  cooperation’:  regulatory,  economic,  and  geopolitical 

 
8 
divergences  (particularly  in  trade  liberalization,  energy  governance,  and  crisis 

management) have created overlapping but sometimes conflicting agendas. The TTIP 

negotiations,  while  framed  as  a  unifying  project,  revealed  underlying  competition 

over  market  access,  regulatory  standards,  and  global  influence.  Thus,  although  the 

U.S.  and  EU  maintain  a  partnership  for  mutual  strategic  benefit,  they are not driven 

solely by full dominance of the U.S. and blind integration in all terms. 

However,  there  are  not  many  studies  addressing  in  particular  the  U.S.  role  in 

energy  markets  and  its  influence  on  Russia  and  EU  cooperation  with  Russia  in  the 

energy  sphere.  There  is  extensive  research  on  the  U.S.  foreign  policy,  on  the 

formation of global energy markets, external power projection of the United States, on 

the  European  Union,  literature  assessing  EU-Russia  energy  relations,  dependency, 

securitisation and evolution. Despite this, there remains a significant research gap as 

there  is  no  comprehensive  study  that  would  combine  these  factors  together  to  show 

how  in  fact  the  presence of the U.S. shaped energy markets and Russia-EU relations 

in  particular  in  a  given  period  and  how  this  process has unfold. While many studies 

have  focused  on  the  broader  geopolitical  implications  of  U.S.  sanctions  and 

diplomatic  efforts,  less  attention  has  been  paid  to  how  these  external  pressures  are 

balanced  with  the  EU's  strategic  interests.  Moreover,  the  impact  of  current  hybrid 

warfare  (after  2022)  has  not  been  thoroughly  explored  in  existing  literature. 

Therefore,  this  study  fills  this  research  gap, examining specific mechanisms through 

which  U.S.  influence  was  projected  on  energy  markets  and  how  it  has  shaped  EU 

energy policies, taking into account both external and internal factors.  

The timeframe of the study is the period from 2008 to 2024 — beginning with 

shale revolution in the USA and capturing presidencies of Bush Jr. (one year in 2008), 

Obama,  Trump  and  Biden  in  the  U.S.  and three crises in EU-Russia relations (2009, 

2014, 20221 , as well as consequences of the 2008 economic crisis and global energy 

1 2009 crisis — Russia-Ukraine dispute over gas prices and unpaid debts, when Russia cut off gas supplies to Ukraine, 
affecting  gas  delivery  to  several  European  countries  for  nearly  two  weeks;  2014  crisis  —  Crimean  referendum  and 

 
9 
crisis  related  to  the pandemic. This timeframe allows to show change of U.S. energy 

policy through times of these crises. 

The  hypothesis  is  the  following:  A  combination  of  soft  and  hard  power 

instruments  in  the  US  foreign  policy  and  energy  strategy  supported  the  EU’s  shift 

from  cooperation  with  Russia  to  diversification  of  energy  supplies,  securitization  of 

Russian  energy  and  demonization  of  Russia  as  a  whole,  and  to  eventual  change  in 

relations with Russian Pivot to Asia and EU turn to imports of U.S LNG, from which 

the  USA  benefited  the  most.  But  the  U.S.  influence  was not a primary deterministic 

one — own interests of the European Union and lack of unified internal opinion also 

matter. 

The  methodology  is  constructed  to  allow  for  comprehensive  analysis  of  all 

necessary factors, combining qualitative and quantitative methods to catch underlying 

political  and  strategic  narratives,  while  also  providing  enough  statistical  empirical 

evidence.  International  relations  theories  of  neoclassical  realism,  neoliberalism  and 

constructivism will also be provided as frameworks for analysis. 

Regarding  the  theoretical  framework,  in  this  research  neoclassical  realism, 

constructivism  (securitization  theory),  and  neoliberalism  (complex  interdependence) 

theories  are  used. Neoclassical realism is best to explain foreign policies of the USA 

and the EU, as it focuses on the interplay between the international system’s pressures 

and  domestic  factors  in  shaping  an  actor's  behavior2.  Unlike  classical  realism,  that 

does not pay any attention to internal processes within state, and sees the world as an 

arena of wars due to human nature, and structural realism that sees reasons of wars in 

changes of balance of power, neoclassical realism is also looking inside the state, with 

broadened view on domestic processes which is extremely important in the case of the 

U.S. and the EU policy formation and development of state behavior. This shows how 

reunification  with  Russia  was  severely  condemned  by  the  EU;  2022  —  Russia-Ukraine  crisis  also  followed  by  EU 
accusations of Russia as an aggressor. 
2 Rose, G. (1998). Neoclassical realism and theories of foreign policy. World Politics, 51(1), 144–172  

 
10 
EU  member  states  balance  external  threats  with domestic political priorities, leading 

to a better understanding of the EU's energy policy evolution. The U.S. foreign policy 

formulation  and  implementation  is  also  a  combination  of  domestic  pressures  and 

needs and the defence of national interest. Constructivism allows better exploring how 

U.S.  narratives,  discourses  and  personalities  have  shaped  the  energy  market and EU 

perceptions  of  Russia  as  well  as  the  EU  shaped  it. This adds depth by showing how 

ideational  factors, identity, norms, perceptions and narratives shaped energy relations 

in  construction  of  reality  —  not  just material interests. Securitization theory, being a 

part  of  a  Copenhagen  school  of  IR  and  of  the  constructivist  paradigm,  helps  to 

illustrate  how  the  U.S.  has  framed  Russia  as  a  security  threat,  influencing  EU 

discourse  and  policy  and  how  this  process  unfolded  with  particular  extraordinary 

measures used and how in fact energy is framed as a security threat. Finally, complex 

interdependence theory, which is a part of neoliberal paradigm, allows to demonstrate 

and  explain  deep  economic  ties  between  Russia  and  the  EU,  where  mutual  energy 

dependence  created  both  cooperation and vulnerability. It also captures how the U.S. 

influence  worked  through  markets  and  economies.  Together  these  theories  offer  a 

comprehensive  lens  to  understand  both  strategic  behavior  and  evolving  threat 

perceptions in the U.S.-Russia-EU energy dynamics. 

As  for  the  methods,  this  research  uses  a  mixed-method  approach.  Qualitative 

methods  of  discourse  and  content  analysis  are  used  to  collect  and  analyse 

non-numerical  data  with  deduction,  induction  and  synthesis.  And  quantitative 

descriptive  statistical  analysis  will  be  used  to  assess  numerical  data  and  statistical 

evidence.  To  assess  the  narrative  and  propaganda  efforts  by  the  U.S.,  the  work  will 

analyze public statements with discourse analysis. This method allows to see how key 

actors  communicate  meanings  around  energy  and  security,  especially  in moments of 

crisis  and  tension.  Analysis  of  written  and  spoken  language  of  decision-makers  and 

influential  officials,  speeches  and  official  statements  of  the  U.S.  representatives  is 

helpful  to  analyse their tools and techniques to reveal how Russia was portrayed and 

 
11 
how  it  was  translated  and  accepted  by  the  EU.  The  study  explores  implicit 

mechanisms and the role of publicly available information in shaping perceptions and 

policy  directions.  It  is  possible to reveal how the U.S. LNG was framed as ‘freedom 

gas’ and Russian as ‘evil’ and coercion. This is useful combined with the framework 

of  securitization  theory  to  follow  the  ways  in  which  language  was  used  to  bring 

attention to the issue of energy dependency and justify reaction.  

Quantitative methods aim to assess EU-Russia, EU-U.S. energy trade data, with 

a  focus  on  LNG  imports  —  analysis  of  trade  volumes,  trends,  and  shifts  in  energy 

supply sources. Descriptive statistical analysis of changes in trade volumes and prices 

of  different  energy  types  serves  to  identify  patterns  between  U.S.  policy  shifts  and 

changes in energy markets, in the EU in particular. Sanctions data is used to evaluate 

their  impact  on  Russian  energy  exports  and  EU  energy  imports  —  with  a  focus  on 

changes  in  supply  and  the  effectiveness  of  sanctions  and  their  implications  for  EU 

energy security (in particular in times of 2014 and 2022 Ukraine crises are analysed to 

reveal external shocks’ role and U.S. influence). Statistical methods are important for 

providing credible empirical evidence for key trends and shifts, reinforcing qualitative 

findings.  The  radar  chart  is  then  employed  to visually capture and compare multiple 

dimensions  of  U.S.  energy  policies  and  their  impacts  on  global  energy  markets. 

Instruments  revealed  are  assigned  weights  (thus,  their  importance  is  quantified)  and 

then compared with radar chart visualization. This enables the comparison of multiple 

variables in a structured, measurable way.  

For such analysis it is important to choose primary sources that would allow for 

comprehensive  understanding  of  dynamics  of  interest.  The  empirical  basis  for  this 

work includes:  

●  Official  governmental  documents  of  the  U.S.:  All  press  releases, media notes, 

fact sheets, briefing papers, remarks of U.S. officials and departments published 

by the U.S. Department of State, and U.S. Congress and Department of Energy 

 
12 

between 2008 and 2024, where Russia, energy, oil, gas and other related topics 

are mentioned (such as U.S.-EU joint statements and U.S. energy policy reports 

on  LNG  exports)  —  crucial  to  follow  the  change  in  discourse  and  in  general 

interest in the topic, changing alliances and partnership agreements. Statements 

of  Presidents  Barack  Obama,  Donald  Trump  and  Joe  Biden,  as  well  as 

Secretaries  of  State  Hillary  Clinton,  John  Kerry,  Michael  R.  Pompeo  and 

Antony J. Blinken are assessed.  

●  Official  documents  of  the  EU:  The  European  Commission,  the  European 

Council  and  the  Council  of  the  EU  papers  published  in  the  period  of  interest 

that  are  related  to  Russia  and  energy  strategies.  Such  as REPowerEU, Energy 

Union  Plan,  Energy  targets  for  2040  and  statements  of  EU  officials  (Josep 

Borrell, Ursula von der Leyen, Federica Mogherini). 

●  To  a  lesser  extent,  representations  of  speeches  and  events  in  media 

organizations like Reuters and CNBC. 

●  Statistics  and  industry  reports:  Reports  from  the  U.S.  Energy  Information 

Administration  (EIA)  and  documents  from  international  bodies  such  as  the 

International  Energy  Agency  (IEA),  Eurostat,  Oxford  Institute  for  Energy 

Studies  and  the  European  Union  provide  helpful  extensive  quantitative  and 

qualitative  data  that  help  in understanding the economic dimensions of energy 

market changes. 

The  research  is  expected  to  reveal  that  ultimately,  the  U.S.  finds  itself  in  the 

most  advantageous  position  and  with  highest  profit  from  contemporary  changes  in  

energy  market  dynamics.  The  U.S.  strategically  used  energy  sources  as  a  tool  for 

maintaining  power  and  to  benefit  from  it  in  times  of  crises  and  opening  of  new 

markets  that  allow  high  penetration  and  opportunities  for  high  economic  profit with 

improved  political  image.  Since  the  U.S.  shale revolution, American LNG became a 

viable  alternative  to  Russian  gas  in  Europe,  but  it  was  only  under  the  Trump 

administration that the United States began actively promoting its LNG exports to the 

 
13 
EU. The outbreak of Russian Special Military Operation in Ukraine and the resulting 

hybrid  war  with  the  West  forced  the  European  Union  to  sharply  reduce  imports  of 

Russian  gas  and  increase  reliance  on  U.S. LNG — a shift the United States strongly 

encouraged.  However,  American  influence,  while  significant,  was  not  the  primary 

determinant  of  EU  energy  policy.  Prior  to  the  war,  despite  repeated  U.S.  calls  to 

abandon  Russian  gas,  key  EU  member  states  such  as Germany prioritized their own 

strategic  and  economic  interests.  Moreover,  there  was  no  unified position within the 

EU itself regarding energy dependence on Russia. 

The  boundaries  of  this  research  are  defined  by several key limitations. Firstly, 

the  study  primarily  relies  on  publicly  accessible  official  sources,  and  some 

information could be not openly disclosed. While publicly available documents, such 

as  press  releases  and  official  statements, provide valuable insights, they often reflect 

official  narratives,  potentially  limiting  the  depth  of  analysis.  Field  research  and 

practical observation of the work of departments of interest is not possible, due to the 

very  nature  of  the  IR  sphere,  thus,  only  online  search  for  accessible  information  is 

possible,  which  may  limit the basis for analysis. Moreover, geopolitical complexities 

and  shifting  international  alliances  may  affect  the  consistency  of  energy  policies, 

making  it  difficult  to  predict  long-term  trends  and  account  for  additional  external 

factors that influenced the EU, Russia and global markets. 

The  structure  of  this  research  is  the  following:  first  chapter  provides  an 

introduction  into  the  importance  and  global  significance  of  energy  as  a  material 

resource  and  political  leverage,  then  there  will  be  historical  background  of  the  U.S. 

presence  on  global  energy  markets  as  a  major  importer,  followed  by  explanation  of 

the U.S. energy policy formulation.  

Second  chapter  assesses  the  United  States  presence  on  energy  markets from a 

theoretical  standpoint  —  combining  different  IR  theories  to  explain  aspects  of  U.S. 

actions,  as  well  as  the  EU  securitization  of  energy  dependence  on  Russia.  Third 

 
14 
chapter is dedicated to in-depth discourse and content analysis in chronological order. 

Thus, evolution of methods, instruments and mechanisms of U.S. influence on energy 

markets  is  analyzed  and  presented  in  a  table  and  a radar chart. The fourth, and final 

chapter  aims  to assess the current situation around Russia-EU relations and the point 

from  which  Russian  Pivot  to  Asia  starts  with  future  implications  and  policy 

recommendations  for  related  actors.  Thus,  the  work  combines  several  methods  and 

theoretical  frameworks  to  conduct  a  deep  analysis  of  the  role  of  the  USA  in  the 

transformation  of  global  energy  markets  and  its  impact  on  Russia  and  Russia-EU 

relations. 

 
 
15 
CHAPTER 1: THE HISTORY OF THE IMPORTANCE OF ENERGY AND 
THE U.S. ENERGY INFLUENCE  

Oil  is  often  described  as  the  ‘blood  of  the  war’,  ‘blood  of  the  earth’  and  the 

‘lifeblood  of  victory’.  These  metaphors  stand  on  the  basis  of  oil  being  a  reason  for 

many  wars,  economic  competitions,  main  resource  for  war,  economy  of  every  state 

and  the  most  tradeable  asset  in  the  world3  due  to  its  pivotal  role  in  powering 

industries,  transportation,  and  electricity  generation  (OEC,  2023).  Throughout  the 

history  of  the  modern  era,  energy  resources,  and above all oil, have shaped not only 

economic development but also the strategic calculus of great powers. From WWII to 

the  Cold  War  and  contemporary  Arctic  race,  the  quest  for  energy  shaped  alliances, 

triggered  conflicts,  and  reshaped geopolitical boundaries. This chapter will introduce 

foundations  of  the  importance  of  energy  and  its  leverage  as  a  tool  of  power  with 

subsequent historical overview of the usage of this leverage by the USA. 

1.1 The Role of Energy in International Relations 

Collectively, National Oil Companies control approximately 90% of the world’s 

proven oil reserves4 (Visualizing Energy, 2024). Every state in the world needs energy 

to  survive  and  to  be  economically  competitive.  That  is  why  it  is  so  desirable  for 

powerful  states,  such  as  the  USA,  to  maintain  and  develop  influence  in  the  world 

energy market. Russia is a valuable player in this arena, being the second top crude oil 

and natural gas liquids exporter since 1997, after Saudi Arabia5 (IEA, 2021). In 2023 

Russian production of oil accounted for 11% of world total production, twice less than 

the U.S. one6 (EIA, 2024). In general, the United States, Russia and Saudi Arabia can 

be  considered  to  be  the  most  powerful  energy  producers  in  the  world.  This  creates 

3 Observatory of Economic Complexity. (2023). World profile. https://oec.world/en/profile/world/wld  
4  Visualizing  Energy.  (June  10,  2024).  Where  are  the  world’s  most  valuable  oil  and  gas  companies?  Retrieved from 
https://visualizingenergy.org/where-are-the-worlds-most-valuable-oil-and-gas-companies/  
5  International  Energy  Agency.  (July  30,  2021).  World  top  crude  and  NGL  exporters  (1997-2019).  Retrieved  from 
https://www.iea.org/data-and-statistics/charts/world-top-crude-and-ngl-exporters-1997-2019  
6  U.S.  Energy  Information  Administration.  (April  11,  2024).  How  much  carbon  dioxide  is  produced  from  burning 
gasoline and diesel fuel? Retrieved from https://www.eia.gov/tools/faqs/faq.php?id=709&t=6  

 
16 
ground for competition for sales markets, which is evident in Russia-U.S. activities on 

the  European  gas  market,  especially  since  2022,  when  Russian  supplies  were 

significantly  cut  and  the  U.S.  appeared  to  be  the  largest  LNG  exporter  to  the  EU, 

previously  the most important Russian energy partner and largest market for Russian 

energy supplies.  

Energy sources are central to global politics and are not only an economic tool 

and a valuable asset in practical terms, but also a strategic asset. Control over energy 

resources, access to them and their distribution is a source of conflicts, wars, alliances 

and  power  hierarchies.  Oil  embargoes  of  the  1970s,  Russia-Ukraine  gas  disputes 

(2006,  2009,  2014,  2022),  China’s  energy  diplomacy  through  BRI  initiative,  and 

current U.S. post-2008 LNG diplomacy and sanctions against Iran oil sector illustrate 

how energy is for a long time used as a strategic tool and in some cases coercively as 

a weapon. Blondeel et al. (2021) argue that nations controlling vast reserves of fossil 

fuels  or  strategic  infrastructure  are  able  to  leverage  their  position  to  secure 

geopolitical  advantages,  while  those  without  such  control  are  at  risk.  However,  it  is 

not  the  case  that  all  states  with  vast  energy  sources  are  capable  of projecting power 

from  it.  Poor  countries  with  no  opportunities  to  develop  infrastructure  may  become 

victims  of  exploitation  by  more  powerful  states.  Nigeria  is  an  example.  It  has  vast 

reserves  of  oil  but  is  not  able  to  effectively  extract  it,  which  brings  attention  of 

Western  companies  to  the  region.  Thus,  despite  being  Africa’s  largest  oil  producer, 

Nigeria  has  long  been  exploited  by,  for  example,  U.S.’s  Chevron  and  ExxonMobil, 

leading  to  environmental  degradation,  human  rights  abuses  and  finally  the  state 

receiving  less  money  than  foreign  companies  (Martial,  2025).  Factor  of  obtaining 

energy  reserves  is  not  enough  to  be  able to use it strategically. Energy markets must 

not be considered solely as economic domains but as politically contested ones where 

state  and  non-state  actors  are  having  strategic  interests,  projecting  power,  and 

challenging orders. 

 
Energy and geopolitics 

17 

Geopolitics is the study of the influence of geographical factors on politics and 

behavior  of  states.  But  there  is  also  a more specific concept — geopolitics of global 

energy  security,  which,  according  to  Bradshaw  (2009,  p.  1920),  is  the  “influence  of 

geographical  factors,  such  as  the  distribution  of  centres  of  supply  and  demand,  on 

state  and  non-state  actions  to  ensure  adequate,  affordable  and  reliable  supplies  of 

energy”7.  Energy  links  physical  geography  to  world  power  dynamics.  Supply  and 

demand in the international energy system are unevenly distributed, and production is 

regionalized  in  politically  sensitive  regions  like  the  Middle  East,  post-Soviet  states 

and Africa, while demand is high in industrialized and rapidly developing economies. 

Control  of  key  oil  and  gas  reserves,  or  influence  over  chokepoints  like  the  Strait  of 

Hormuz or key pipeline routes in Eurasia, enhances ability to project power or create 

dependencies,  particularly  in  energy-importing  areas.  Thus,  importer  states  are 

concerned  with  securing  supply  chains,  which  increases  the  importance  of  energy 

corridors, and transit states (Bradshaw, 2009, p. 1921). Geography, therefore, does not 

merely constrain energy flows — it dictates who exercises power in the global energy 

order.  Thus,  Russian-European  pipelines  and  transit  states,  especially  Ukraine,  are 

politically contested areas where security, economic interest and bargaining meet.  

Oil  has  long  been  (and  will  continue  to  be)  one  of  the  fundamental  causes  of 

geopolitical  conflict,  particularly  in  politically  volatile  but  resource-abundant 

territories. Though oil was an emerging factor in early 20th-century conflict, in World 

War  II  its  strategic  importance  became  highly  present  —  powering  mechanized 

armies,  air  power,  and  navies  required  energy  to  function.  Klare  warns  that  the  US 

presence  in  the  Persian  Gulf,  the  Caspian,  and  Central  Asia,  particularly  following 

9/11 interventions, is intimately linked with energy security imperatives and access to 

oil  shipping  routes.  More  broadly,  the  central  place  of  oil  in  the  global  economy 

7 Bradshaw, M. (2009). The geopolitics of global energy security. Geography Compass, 3(5), 1920–1937  

 
18 
makes  it  a  chronic  cause  of  conflict  (Klare,  2002).  This  is  in  fact  true  regarding 

modern  international  conflict  developments.  From  the  US  invasion  of  Iraq,  often 

explained  through  expression  of  strategic  interest  in  accessing  oil  reserves,  to  civil 

wars  in  Libya  and  South  Sudan,  where  conflicting  forces  fighted  for  access  to  oil. 

Mineral  resources  may  fund  armed  aggression  and  become  bargaining  chips.  Some 

authors  and  Western  media  even  claim  access  to  Ukrainian  coal  deposits  located  in 

Donbas  to be a critical factor of the 2022 Russia-initiated Special Military Operation 

in  Ukraine  (Muggah,  2025;  Polityuk,  2024).  This  demonstrates  that  energy  sources 

are a perpetual source of power struggles. 

The  Second  World  War  marked  the  first  full-scale  global conflict in which oil 

appeared  to  be  the  indispensable  fuel  of  war,  central  to  both  strategy  and  logistics. 

Access  to  petroleum  became  a  key  determinant  of  military  mobility  and  industrial 

resilience.  Both  Japan  and  Germany  launched  campaigns  to  power  their  war 

machines.  Japan,  whose  economy  was  heavily  dependent  on  U.S.  imports  of  oil 

before  the  war  (in  the  late  1930s,  80%  came  from  the  U.S.)8,  viewed  American 

embargoes  in  1941  as  an  immediate  threat  to  national  survival  and  invaded  Pearl 

Harbor  to guarantee alternative supply from the Dutch East Indies (Yergin, 1991, pp. 

314).  Similarly,  German  war  economy  relied  on  synthetic  fuels,  this  was  the  reason 

for  the  Nazi  drive  into  the  Soviet  Union  during  1941  —  particularly  towards  the 

Caucasus  region  —  to  seize  the  Baku  oil  fields  since  Germany’s  synthetic  fuel 

production  and  Romanian  imports were insufficient for a protracted conflict (Yergin, 

1991, pp. 334). Hitler’s 1942 campaign (Operation Blau) prioritized capturing Baku’s 

oil  over  taking  Moscow,  highlighting  fuel’s  pivotal  role  in  German  war  planning. 

Finally,  fuel  shortages  undermined  the  effectiveness  of  Axis  operations  and 

Blitzkrieg’s  momentum,  harming  mobility  and  aviation.  While  the  U.S.,  being 

petroleum-rich and rapidly industrializing, emerged as the world’s largest producer of 

8 Yergin, D. (1991). The prize: The epic quest for oil, money, and power (p. 307). Simon & Schuster.  

 
19 
military  gasoline  —  enabling  the  Allied  side  to  continue  operating  on  both  the 

European  and  Pacific  fronts  (Yergin,  1991,  pp.  371).  Thus,  ability  to  obtain and use 

oil emerged as a structural determinant of World War II victory or defeat. 

The  1973  oil  crisis  appeared  to  be  a  milestone  change  in  world’s  energy, 

brought  by  OAPEC (Organization of Arab Petroleum Exporting Countries) members 

that  imposed  a  ban  on  oil  exports  to  the  U.S.  in retaliation for US support for Israel 

during the Yom Kippur War. Reduction of oil shipments and production rates hurt the 

United  States. It raised major concerns about energy dependency, revealed the U.S.’s 

vulnerability  as  a  net  oil  importer,  and  politicized  oil  for  the  first  time  on  a  global 

scale.  It  led  to  quadrupling  the  price  of  oil  inflicting  a  shock  in  inflation,  industrial 

slow-down,  and  recession  (Bradshaw,  2009,  p.  1924).  The  crisis 

induced 

institutionalization  of  strategic  petroleum  reserves,  the  establishment  of  the 

International  Energy  Agency,  and  the  move  of  energy  policy  to  the  national interest 

level  in  the  USA.  These  developments  illustrate  that  energy  has  functioned  as  a 

central  axis  of  geopolitical  strategy  and  conflict,  making  control  over  resources, 

transit routes, and supply security a defining feature of international power dynamics 

and foreign policy behavior for years. 

Energy as economic leverage and tool of political pressure 

As  it  can  be  seen,  the  control  of  supply  routes  and  price  manipulations 

mechanisms  are  strong  instruments  of  influencing  behavior  of  states  in  the 

international  arena.  Russia’s  gas  supply  disruptions9  are  seen  by  some  authors  as 

examples  of  use  of  power  and  energy  as  a  weapon  (Stern,  2006,  pp.  10-11).  These 

disruptions  influenced  Ukraine’s  energy  security  and  EU’s  downstream  concerns, 

illustrating  how  interdependence  can  be  strategically  exploited.  This  pattern  of 

behavior  reflects  a  realist  logic  where  energy  becomes  a  tool  of  coercion  within 

asymmetric  interdependencies.  In  addition  to  coercion,  energy  is  a  significant 

9 2006 and 2009 gas crises when Russia cut off supplies to Ukraine, causing gas shortages in Europe 

 
20 
instrument  of  ‘resource  diplomacy’  —  diplomatic  utilization  of  energy  commerce, 

investment,  and  infrastructure  to  deepen  friendships  and  expand  geopolitical 

influence.  China’s  Belt  and  Road  Initiative  (BRI),  for  example,  includes  massive 

energy infrastructure projects such as pipelines, ports, and electricity grids across Asia 

and  Africa  designed  to  ensure  long-term  supply  and  support  political  ties.  Energy 

sector was the biggest in investments in early years, making five Chinese construction 

companies  the  largest  globally,  including  PowerChina  (focused  on  construction  of 

energy infrastructure) (IISS, 2022). With similar logic, the United States has promoted 

its  liquefied  natural  gas  exports  as  both  an  economic  opportunity  and  a  geopolitical 

alternative  to  Russian  gas  in  Europe,  especially  under  the  Trump  and  Biden 

administrations.  Thus,  energy  also  functions  as  a  diplomatic  tool supporting broader 

strategic  objectives.  Sanctions targeting energy sectors are a very common feature of 

the U.S. foreign policy and are used to weaken adversaries by restricting their access 

to global markets and technological resources. The U.S. has imposed sweeping energy 

sanctions  on  oil-producing  countries  such  as  Iran,  Venezuela,  and  Russia in order to 

limit  state  revenues  funding  strategic  or  military  activities.  United  States  and 

European  Union  sanctions  on  Iranian  crude  oil  exports  caused  Iran’s  oil  exports  to 

decline  by  approximately  50%  by  201410,  reducing  government revenues by billions 

of dollars each year and placing extreme fiscal pressure on Iran that helped drive Iran 

to  the  negotiating  table,  culminating  in  the  Joint  Comprehensive  Plan  of  Action 

(JCPOA) in 2015, aimed at limiting Iranian nuclear program.  

This  proves  the  ability  and  utility  of  using  energy  sanctions  as  elements  of 

coercive  diplomacy.  However,  this  is  not  what  can  be  said  about  U.S.  and  EU 

sanctions  against  Russia  following  the  2022  special  military  operation  in  Ukraine 

which targeted both crude oil and natural gas technologies, aiming to erode Moscow’s 

long-term  capacity to fund the ‘war’ and maintain its dominance in European energy 

10 U.S. Energy Information Administration. (2015, June 24). Under sanctions, Iran’s crude oil exports have nearly halved 
in three years. https://www.eia.gov/todayinenergy/detail.php?id=21792  

 
21 
markets.  These  measures  were  designed  to  reduce  the  Kremlin’s  hydrocarbon 

revenues  —  which  constituted  45%11  of  federal  budget  income  prior  to  the  conflict 

(IEA,  2022)  —  and  to  undermine  Russia’s  long-term  capacity to finance its military 

operations and sustain its dominant position in the European energy market (European 

Council, 2025). Overall effectiveness of these sanctions is not clear, they did not stop 

the  conflict  and  did  not  undermine  Russia  as  a  powerful  state,  despite  lowering  the 

respect  of  Western  countries.  This  gave  Russia  the  opportunity  to  develop  internal 

production  and  technologies,  find  new  trade  partners  and  develop  previous 

partnerships.  However,  EU  energy  imports  from  Russia  have  indeed  lowered.  Data 

from the Council of the European Union (2025) reveals a significant decrease in EU’s 

reliance on Russian natural gas with its share in EU imports dropping from over 40% 

in 2021 to about 11% in 202412. And U.S. supplies of LNG doubled from 202113 being 

equal  to  45%14  of  total  LNG  imports  in  the  EU  in  2024.  Therefore,  these  sanctions 

reflect  the  integration  of  energy  into  broader  coercive  strategies  in  international 

relations, being both the aim and the instrument.  

Main energy sources overview  

Energy  transitions  refer  to  global  shifts  in  dominant  energy  sources  with  the 

aim to use the most efficient ones. According to Smil (2017, p. 395), there have been 

three  main  energy  transitions  in  history:  coal  replacing  wood  in  the  19th  century 

Industrial  Revolution  due to steam engines and urbanization, then from coal to oil in 

the  20th  century  due  to  development  of  automobiles  and  mechanized  warfare,  then 

from  oil  to  natural  gas  driven  by  its  utilization  for  cooking,  heating  and  maritime 

transportation since it is cleaner burning fuel than coal and oil. Introduction of nuclear 

the  European  Union.

low-carbon  energy  renewables  (solar,  wind  and  hydropower)  pushed  by  ideological 

commitment to climate change mitigation and political agendas. This transition differs 

from  previous  ones  in  the  fact  that it is more politicized and driven primarily not by 

efficiency  (as  these  are  costly  and  more  risky  energy  sources)  but  more  by  political 

considerations  and  international  agreements.  Despite  rising  interest  in  renewables

remains  the  most  internationally  traded  commodity  worldwide, primarily fueling the 

transportation  sector  (IEA,  2024,  p.  19).  Coal’s  share  of  primary  energy has been in 

structural decline since 2008, but it is still extensively used for electricity production16 

(with  China  and  India  being  largest  consumers)  (IEA,  2024).  Natural  gas  is 

extensively used for electricity generation and heating and became more important as 

a ‘transition fuel’ (IEA, 2024, p. 24) replacing coal due to lower carbon emissions. In 

2023 the USA became the largest LNG producer in the world and also produced more 

crude  oil  this  year  than  every  country  did  before  (IEA,  2024,  p.  250).  Thus,  with 

geopolitical  disturbances  after  2022,  which  accelerated  the  shift  away  from  Russian 

hydrocarbons 

in  Europe  and  enhanced  investment  in  renewables  and  LNG 

infrastructure, the United States reaffirmed itself as a global energy supplier.

1.2 The U.S. in Global Energy Markets: An Overview (Pre-2008 Context)  

It  is  important  to  briefly  outline  the  role  of  the  United States in global energy 

markets  prior  to  2008  to  understand  patterns  that  were  present  historically  in  the 

behavior  of  the  state.  The  history  of  high  penetration  of  the  U.S.  in  world  energy 

markets begins with the creation of the petrodollar system. In general, the energy path 

of the country was shaped by a long-standing dependence on foreign oil and strategic 

energy  policymaking.  Early  energy  policies,  both  domestic  and  foreign, had aims of 

securing  access  to  resources  and  stabilizing  markets,  rooting  energy  deeply  in  U.S. 

foreign policy making.  

1970s oil crisis and the establishment of petrodollar system 

The  first  oil  well  in  the  USA,  21  meters  deep,  was  drilled  in  Pennsylvania  in 

1859,  according  to  the  Higher  School  of  Innovative  Business  of  MSU.  It  had  a 

production capacity of 15 barrels of oil per day. In 1870 John D. Rockefeller founded 

the  Standard  Oil  Company  and  became  the  richest  man  of  all  time  with  a  highly 

effective  vertical integration strategy and full control over the supply chain. By 1973 

‘Seven  Sisters’  —  mainly  American  major  oil  companies17  controlled  85%  of  all 

extracted  oil  in  the  Earth18.  The  Organization  of  the  Petroleum  Exporting  Countries 

(OPEC)  was  created  in  1960  —  as  a  response  to  growing  Western  multinational  oil 

company  dominance  over  oil  prices  and  production.  The  majors  dominated  the  oil 

market and determined prices while countries-producers of oil had little access to their 

resources and revenues. Discontented with this inability to control their oil resources, 

five  founding  members  —  oil-exporting  nations  —  decided  to  unite  to  protect  their 

economic  interests  (OPEC,  n.d.).  The  OPEC  attempted  to  reduce  dependence  on 

Western  oil  companies  and  enhance  the  negotiating  power  of  member  states.  Their 

17 Esso Petroleum, Standard Oil of New York (later Mobil), Standard Oil of California (later Chevron), Texaco, Royal 
Dutch Shell, and British Petroleum 
18 
new 
and 
https://oilandgascourses.org/the-new-seven-supermajor-oil-companies/  

supermajor 

companies. 

Courses. 

(n.d.). 

seven 

Gas 

The 

Oil 

oil 

 
24 
success was evident during the 1973 oil crisis, when OPEC reduced oil production to 

increase  oil  prices  under  anti-Western  political  objectives.  The 1970s were a turning 

point  for  global  energy  markets.  Following  the  1973  oil  crisis,  the  U.S.  entered  an 

agreement with Saudi Arabia to denominate oil sales in U.S. dollars. This was a signal 

of  the  establishment  of  the  petrodollar  system,  backing  the  dollar’s  dominance  of 

international  markets  and  ensuring  U.S.  economic  influence  across  the  globe.  It  is 

important to trace back to the Bretton Woods system, which collapsed in 1971. It was 

agreed in 1944 and brought the return of the Gold Standard — pegged the U.S. dollar 

to gold and the other currencies to the dollar. U.S. fiscal policy, particularly financing 

military  for  the  war  in  Vietnam  and  domestic  social  welfare  programs,  created 

increasing inflation and a sharp reduction in the U.S. gold reserves by the late 1960s. 

President  Nixon,  in  August  1971,  announced  that  convertibility of the dollar to gold 

had  to  be  suspended,  which  marked  the  end  of  the  Bretton  Woods  system.  This 

provoked  global  uncertainty  over  the  worth  of  the  dollar  and  its  status  as  world 

currency.  The  dollar  needed  another  device  by  which  to  still  control  world  demand. 

That device was oil.  

Massive geopolitical crisis in October 1973 was provoked after the Yom Kippur 

War  —  between  Israel  (supported  by  the  USA)  and  a  coalition  of  Arab  states, 

primarily  Egypt  and  Syria.  OAPEC  members imposed an oil embargo on the United 

States  and  allies  for  support  of  Israel  (Reich,  1995).  Due  to  reduced  supply  options 

and  stable  demand,  oil  prices  rose  from  $3  before  the  crisis  to  over  $11  a  barrel  in 

January  1974,  causing  a  global  recession  (Merrill,  2007).  For  the  United  States,  the 

crisis  was  a  turning  point  that  confirmed  the  strategic  importance  of  controlling 

energy  resources  and  protecting  the  dollar’s  hegemony.  In  1974,  the  U.S.  made  a 

historic agreement with Saudi Arabia — the world’s top oil producer — on which the 

petrodollar  system  was  based.  Negotiated  by  Henry  Kissinger,  this  secret  deal 

required  Saudi  Arabia  to  sell  oil  in  American  dollars  alone.  The  oil-exporting 

 
25 
countries’  dollar  surpluses  were  invested  in  U.S.  government  bonds,  financial 

markets,  and  infrastructure.  In  exchange  for  payment,  the  U.S.  guaranteed  Saudi 

Arabia  and  other  Gulf  states  political  stability  and  military  protection.  This  treaty 

provided  the  United  States  with a series of significant benefits. Countries around the 

globe  needed  dollars  to  purchase  oil,  making  the  dollar  the  world  reserve  currency. 

Recycling  petrodollars  back  into  the  United  States  provided  it  with  a  steady  capital 

inflow to use in financing trade deficits and defense. The U.S. also secured substantial 

control  over  international  oil  markets  and  OPEC  policy.  As  a  result of the oil crisis, 

Congress created the Strategic Petroleum Reserve (SPR) in 1975 as insurance against 

future  supply  disruption.  It was an emergency source of oil to mitigate the impact of 

future  supply  shocks  that  enhanced  U.S.  negotiating  leverage  by  serving  as  a buffer 

against  external  supply  pressure.  It also helped stabilize world markets by selling oil 

during  periods of high price volatility. The SPR was designed to store up to 1 billion 

barrels of crude oil in Gulf Coast salt caverns. Although it never approached operation 

at  full  capacity,  it  was  a  key  component  of  U.S.  energy  security.  In  contrast,  the 

International  Energy  Agency  (IEA)  was  created  in  1974  and  consisted  of  31  states 

representing  75%  of  global  energy  consumption  (IEA,  n.d.).  Organization  aimed  to 

coordinate  energy  policies  among  major  oil-consuming  nations  and  make  them  less 

vulnerable  to  supply  shocks.  The  IEA  provided  reliable  information  and  forecasts 

regarding  global  energy  markets and helped member countries to reduce dependence 

on  foreign  oil  and  adopt  more  sustainable  energy  policies.  During  this  period,  the 

Soviet Union was also a significant oil exporter, but the emergence of the petrodollar 

system  limited  its  ability  to  bypass  Western  financial  institutions.  The  growing 

involvement of the U.S. in global energy markets set the stage for future competition 

between the two superpowers. 

The  petrodollar  system  solidified  the  dollar position in international trade, and 

it  became  the  de  facto  global  reserve  currency.  Oil-importing  countries  became 

 
26 
dollar-reserve  dependent,  and  subjected  to  currency  risks  and  U.S.  monetary  policy. 

For the Soviet Union, U.S. dollar supremacy in global energy markets proved to be a 

challenge. These weaknesses became more pronounced in the 1980s and set the stage 

for  competition  in  the  future  between  Russia  and  the  U.S. in global energy markets, 

particularly in Europe. 

The 1990s and early 2000s: episodic engagement 

These  years  witnessed  episodic  U.S.  engagement  in  the  energy  sector,  driven 

largely  by  geopolitical  considerations.  The  collapse  of  the  Soviet  Union  in  1991 

proved  the  U.S.  to  remain  the  only  superpower  in  the  international  arena.  This 

position  required  more  active  policies, and a shift in foreign focus from containment 

of  communism  to  securing global energy resources and ensuring regional stability in 

key  oil-producing  areas.  The  Gulf  War  (1990-1991)  was  an  example  of  how energy 

and  military  intervention  were  interconnected.  The  war  was  triggered  by  Iraq’s 

invasion  of  Kuwait  —  Iraq  gained  control  of  approximately  20%  of  the  world’s  oil 

reserves,  raising  concerns  about  global  energy  security  (History,  2024).  The U.S., at 

the head of a coalition of countries in Operation Desert Storm, quickly drove out Iraqi 

troops  and  restored  Kuwaiti  sovereignty.  Objective  was  to  prevent  Iraq  from 

monopolizing  the  Gulf’s  oil  supply,  which  could  give  it  significant  leverage  over 

global  markets.  The  intervention  reinforced  the  U.S.  commitment  to  maintaining 

security  in  the  Gulf  region  and  protecting  its energy interests, strengthening military 

alliances  and  making  permanent  U.S.  military  bases  in  the  region.  It  also  allowed 

American  oil  majors  to  expand  their  influence  in  the  region,  particularly  in  Saudi 

Arabia and Kuwait (Klare, 2004). The U.S. also gained control over regional politics, 

using  its  presence  to  contain  Iraq and dictate strategic direction of key oil-producing 

nations,  securing  its  hegemony over world energy and regional geopolitics for future 

decades. There was also an opening of vast energy resources in the Caspian region in 

the  1990s  due  to  the  collapse  of  the  Soviet  Union  and  subsequent  liberalization  of 

 
27 
policies  in  newly  independent  states.  These  conditions created new opportunities for 

the  U.S.  — it promoted the establishment of new export routes to bypass Russia and 

Iran, such as the Baku-Tbilisi-Ceyhan pipeline. The intention was to diversify energy 

supplies,  reduce  Russian  control  over  post-Soviet  supplies  and  reduce  Europe’s 

dependence  on  Russia.  The Caspian region appeared to be a promising alternative to 

Middle  Eastern  oil.  By  investing  in  infrastructure  and  building  alliances  with 

countries  like  Azerbaijan  and  Georgia,  the  U.S.  sought  to  ensure  greater  energy 

security for itself and its allies. However, for Russia these actions were a challenge for 

the  historically  important  geopolitical  sphere  of  influence,  reducing  Russia’s 

monopoly  on  Caspian  oil  exports,  and  forcing  it  to  seek  new  markets  and  build 

alternative  export  routes  (as  the  Nord  Stream).  The  U.S.  efforts  to  reduce  European 

dependency on Russian gas sowed the seeds of future competition in the LNG sector. 

While  the  1990s  were  a  period  of  relative  weakness  for  Russia,  its  power  was 

reinforced  in  the  early  2000s,  particularly  through  the  consolidation  of  Gazprom’s 

control over key gas export routes, including   asset swaps with EU energy companies 

and  geo-economic  strategies.  After  the  1990s, the United States reduced its overseas 

military  activities  outside  of  its  bases in NATO countries, East Asia, and the Persian 

Gulf  as  there  were  no  significant  geopolitical opponents. However, in the 2000s, the 

U.S.  military  operations  in  the Middle East and Afghanistan increased, accompanied 

with new access to military infrastructure in Central Asian states, and new presence in 

Africa and the Arctic.  

The early 2000s marked the beginning of a gradual shift in United States energy 

policy.  While  the  1990s  had  focused  on  regional  conflicts  and  infrastructure 

development, the early 2000s saw a broader, more strategic engagement in the global 

energy  markets.  This  period  was  also  marked  by  rising  concerns  over  U.S.  energy 

dependency  and  the  growing importance of natural gas. Advances in technology and 

the  discovery  of  vast  shale  gas  reserves  in  the  United  States  set  the  stage  for future 

 
28 
shale revolution. This shift reduced its Middle East oil dependence and positioned the 

U.S.  to  be  a  long-term  worldwide  natural  gas  market  leader.  The  terrorist  attacks of 

September  11,  2001,  and  2003  Iraq 

intervention  further  underscored 

the 

vulnerabilities  of  relying  on  foreign  energy  supplies.  This  led  to  increased  calls  for 

energy  independence  and  greater  investment  in  domestic  energy  resources  (Klare, 

2004).  Hurricane  Katrina  (2005)  damaged  a  significant  part  of  oil  platforms  in  the 

Mexican Gulf. Oil prices increased.  

Overall,  unlike  the  intense  and strategic engagement seen during the 1970s oil 

crisis or the aggressive push for dominance after the shale revolution, this period was 

characterized  by selective and episodic interventions driven primarily by geopolitical 

interests rather than comprehensive energy strategies. U.S. involvement in the energy 

sector  during  these  years  was  driven  by  regional  conflicts,  post-Cold  War  resource 

competition,  and  efforts  to  diversify  energy  supply  routes  to  reduce  dependency  on 

politically volatile regions. 

 
 
1.3 The U.S. Energy Policy Formation 

29 

To  explain  the  influence  of  the  U.S.  globally  in  the  sphere  of  energy,  it  is 

important  to  understand  how  the  U.S.  energy policy is formulated, implemented and 

controlled.  It  is  formulated  via  a  decentralized,  multi-layered  process  between  the 

legislative,  executive,  and 

judicial  branches  of  government  and  significant 

independent agencies and private sector influence.  

Speaking  about  the  legislative  branch,  represented  by  Congress,  it  plays  a 

central  role  in  debating,  drafting  and  passing  laws,  including those related to energy 

and environmental standards and foreign relations. Main U.S. energy laws are Energy 

Policy  Act  of  2005  (Pub.  L.  No.  109-58,  2005)19  and  the  Energy  Independence  and 

Security  Act  of  2007  (Pub.  L.  No.  110-140,  2007)20.  They  establish  targets  for 

renewable  energy, energy efficiency, and modernization of energy infrastructure. The 

Energy Policy Act of 2005 is a landmark expansion of U.S. energy policy with direct 

effects  both  on  domestic  production and the emerging role in global energy markets. 

The  Act  was  signed  into  law  on  August  8,  2005, and authorized $14.5 billion in tax 

credits  designed  to  boost  domestic  energy  production  across several industry sectors 

such as oil, natural gas, coal, nuclear energy, and renewables. In the oil and gas sector, 

it  authorized  subsidies  for  offshore  drilling,  mandated  leasing  federal  lands,  and 

accelerated  environmental  review  of  energy  projects,  thus  accelerating  fossil  fuel 

development.  It  also  promoted  the  construction  of  LNG  terminals  by  granting  the 

Federal  Energy  Regulatory  Commission  (FERC)  exclusive  authority to license LNG 

facilities  (Sec.  311),  a  critical  action which later enabled the U.S. LNG export boom 

after  2016.  Additionally,  the  Act  created  a  framework  for  promoting  biofuels, 

requesting  7.5 billion gallons of renewable fuels to be blended into gasoline by 2012 

(Renewable Fuel Standard, Sec. 1501). By promoting a diverse mix of energy sources

30 
and fostering infrastructure development, the Energy Policy Act of 2005 laid the basis 

for  the  U.S.  shale  revolution  and  the  USA  rise  as  a  leading  energy  exporter.  The 

Energy  Independence  and  Security  Act  of  2007  aimed  at  reducing  U.S.  dependence 

on  oil  and  promoting  sustainability  in  energy  consumption.  Signed  into  law  on 

December  19,  2007,  it  raised  Corporate  Average  Fuel  Economy  (CAFE)  standards 

significantly,  mandating  that  new  cars  and  light  trucks  achieve  35  miles  per  gallon 

(mpg)  by  2020,  up  from  the  previous  average  of  27.5  mpg.  It  also  raised  the 

Renewable  Fuel  Standard  (RFS),  requiring  the  blending  of  36  billion  gallons  of 

biofuels  annually  by  2022,  up  from  7.5  billion  gallons  previously  mandated  in  the 

2005  Energy  Policy  Act.  The  Act  also  promoted  energy  efficiency  in  buildings  and 

appliances  and  provided  funding  for  research  into  advanced  battery technologies for 

electric  vehicles.  EISA  reflected  strategic  reorientation  to  energy  security  via 

diversification  and  climate-oriented  policies  that  shaped  the  framework  of  U.S. 

foreign 

energy 

interactions,  particularly 

in 

strengthening 

clean 

energy 

innovation-based  alliances  and  in  underpinning  the  legitimacy  of  U.S.  leadership 

within  global  climate  negotiations.  Latest 2022 Inflation Reduction Act (Pub. L. No. 

117-169) is the most climate-focused bill ever enacted into law in American history. It 

is  set  to  invest  approximately  $369  billion  on  clean  energy  development  and 

emissions  reduction  programs  over  ten years. It includes across-the-board tax credits 

to  encourage  installation  of  renewable  energy,  including  extended  credits  for  solar, 

wind,  battery storage, and clean hydrogen projects. The Act also provides significant 

incentives  to  purchase  electric  vehicles  and  to  encourage  the  building  of  carbon 

capture  and  storage  (CCS)  technology.  In  addition  to  national  policy,  the  IRA  was 

aimed to strengthen American credibility at global climate negotiations.  

As  for  the  executive  branch,  which  includes president and executive agencies, 

the  president  sets  overall  direction,  particularly  in  foreign  energy  policy,  through 

directives,  orders  and  national  strategies.  For  this  research  Obama’s,  Biden’s  and 

 
31 
Trump’s  actions  are  assessed, with influence of personality on foreign energy policy, 

which  is  strong  in  the  USA  as  there  is  in  fact  a  strong  role  of  president  and  his 

direction  and  strategic  view.  It  is  evident  in how easily Trump (in recent 2025 term) 

blocked  over  70  climate  and  green  energy  initiatives  of  Biden’s  administration,  for 

example,  bans  on  new  offshore  drilling,  exiting  the  Paris  Climate  Agreement,  and 

halting  substantial  green  energy  funding.  These  actions  removed  federal  climate 

targets  of  decarbonizing  the  electricity  sector  by  2035  and  achieving  50%  electric 

vehicle sales by 2030 (Williams, 2025). Barack Obama (2009-2017) focused on clean 

energy and climate change, through, for example the Clean Power Plan, while Donald 

Trump  (2017-2021)  emphasized  energy  independence  and  fossil  fuel  development, 

rolling  back  several  environmental  regulations.  And  Joe  Biden  (2021-2025) 

prioritized rejoining the Paris Agreement and passed the IRA to promote clean energy.  

In fact American energy policy reflected the vision of the state leader in each term.  

Key  agencies  responsible  for  energy  policy  formulation  and  implementation 

are:  the  Department  of  Energy  —  nuclear  energy,  efficiency,  Strategic  Petroleum 

Reserve  and  technologies  funding.  Key  figures  were  Steven  Chu  (2009-2013)  who 

advocated  for  renewable  energy  and  energy  efficiency  (as  Obama),  Rick  Perry 

(2017-2019)  who  supported  fossil  fuel  development  and  energy  exports  (same  as 

Trump) and Jennifer Granholm (2021-2025) who focused on clean energy innovation 

and  infrastructure  (same  as  Biden).  Next  agency  is  the  Department  of  State  as  it  is 

responsible  for  energy  diplomacy  (for  example,  for  the  U.S.-EU  Energy Council), it 

integrates  energy  policy  in  broader  state’s  foreign  policy  through  international 

negotiations  and partnerships; Federal Energy Regulatory Commission is responsible 

for   integrating  renewable  grids  and  for  interstate  transmission  of  electricity, natural 

gas, and oil; the Environmental Protection Agency states for environmental impacts of 

energy production — emission standards and assessment.  

 
32 

Federal  courts  enforce  constitutional  and  administrative  law,  for  example, 

ruling  on  environmental  legislation  (e.g.,  West  Virginia  v.  EPA  decision21,  2022, 

limiting  EPA  regulatory  powers).  Whilst  governments  in  states  have  independence 

concerning  energy  production  and  regulation  within  their  territories  (e.g.,  Texas  oil 

extraction  regulations,  California  renewable  energy  legislation).  Utility  and  energy 

companies (like ExxonMobil, Chevron, NextEra Energy) and interest groups (like the 

American  Petroleum  Institute  and  the  Solar  Energy  Industries  Association)  exert 

significant influence through lobbying, litigation, and public pressure. Therefore, U.S. 

domestic  energy  policy  is  not  only  a  response  to  domestic  economic  and  security 

needs  but  also  a  foundation  for  overall international influence. By promoting energy 

diversification,  infrastructure  investment,  and  export  capability,  the  U.S.  became  a 

dominant  energy  supplier  from being a top energy importer. These developments are 

vital for understanding the influence that has been left on global energy markets by its 

energy policy-making.  

In  sum,  this  chapter  has  demonstrated  that  energy  has  been  not  only  an 

economic  necessity  but  also  a  successful  strategic  tool  of  global  geopolitics 

throughout  history.  From  World  War  II  to  the  1973  oil  crisis  and  the  launch  of  the 

petrodollar  system,  the  United  States  has  increasingly  incorporated  energy  security 

into  foreign policy. Consequently, U.S. energy policy has been at the leading edge of 

conflict  dynamics,  alliances,  and  global  market structures. Through the development 

of  domestic  shale  production,  legislative  structures,  and  the  extension  of  LNG 

exports, the U.S. has moved from dependency to dominance in global energy markets.
