CHAPTER 2: THEORETICAL FOUNDATIONS OF U.S. ENERGY POLICY 

PROJECTION AND EU-RUSSIA ENERGY RELATIONS (2008-2024)  

The  transformation  of world energy markets between 2008 and 2024 reformed 

the strategic environment in which the United States, Russia, and the European Union 

interacted  with  one  another.  The  account  for  this  transformation  requires  a  theory 

capable  of  explaining  not  just  modifications  in  material  capacities,  but  shifting 

perceptions, institutions, and interdependencies that constituted energy relations. This 

chapter 

implements 

IR 

theories 

for 

the  explanation:  neoclassical  realism, 

neoliberalism  (complex  interdependence),  and  constructivism  (securitization  theory) 

—  to  create  a  framework  to  examine  U.S. global energy policy and EU-Russian gas 

relations.  Neoclassical  realism  highlights  how  the  United  States  employed  its 

production  of  energy,  especially  since  the  shale  revolution,  to  assert  power  abroad. 

Securitization  theory  and  constructivism  explain  how  energy  was  redefined  as  a 

security  threat,  particularly  Russian  gas  supplies,  and  influenced  both American and 

European  debate.  Neoliberalism  and  complex  interdependence  theory,  however, 

highlight  economic,  institutional,  and  political  ties  that  limited  pure  power 

competition  and  offered  room  for  cooperation. By integrating these perspectives, the 

chapter  argues  that  U.S.  moves  (though  significant)  represented  only  a  part  of  a 

broader  structural  and  political  shift  in  Europe,  with  indigenous  interests,  regulation 

policies, and threat perceptions also critically determining the push away from Russia 

in terms of energy. 

2.1 The Shale Revolution and U.S. Energy Independence as a Strategic Asset 

Neoclassical  realism  provides  a useful explanation of U.S. energy policy since 

2008.  Structural  realist  Kenneth  Waltz  (1959)  discusses  three  ‘images’  of  state 

analysis:  first  image  —  individual  decision-makers,  second  — domestic politics and 

third  —  international.  Neoclassical  realism  accounts  for  second  and  third  images, 

combining  rationale  from  domestic  and  foreign  conditions.  Gideon  Rose  (1998) 

 
34 
formulated  the  concept  of  neoclassical  realism  by  arguing  that  while  international 

structure  limits  states,  foreign  policies  are  filtered  through  domestic perceptions and 

capacities.  Fareed  Zakaria  (1998)  emphasized  that  the  state’s  ability  to  convert 

material  capabilities  into  global  power  is  a  function of its domestic strength, an idea 

performed in the U.S. capacity to convert its shale boom into global energy leverage. 

Randall  Schweller  (1998)  further  built  on  this  by  highlighting  that  states  pursue  not 

only  security  but  also  revisionist  goals  based  on  domestic  politics  — paralleling the 

way  in  which  U.S.  governments  tied  energy  policy  to  broader  strategies  of 

international  alliance  rebalancing  and  limiting  Russia’s  energy  influence  in  Europe. 

Finally, Lobell, Ripsman, and Taliaferro (2009) specify leadership perceptions’ role in 

translating  systemic  incentives  into  concrete  foreign  policies  via  elite consensus and 

state-society  relations.  Thus,  neoclassical  realism  accounts  for  the  way  in  which the 

U.S.,  in  the  face  of  options  made  possible  through  the shale revolution and elevated 

global  energy  prices  generally,  pursued  the exercise of influence within EU’s energy 

markets  but  did  so  on  terms  framed  by  domestic  political  agendas,  institutional 

capabilities, and elite threat visions targeted at Russia. 

The U.S. shale revolution reshaped the global energy landscape. Technological 

advancements  in  hydraulic  fracturing  and  horizontal  drilling turned the U.S. into the 

world’s top oil and natural gas producer. Between 2007 and 2016, U.S. oil production 

increased  by 75%, while natural gas production increased by 39%22 (IPAA.org, n.d.). 

Hydraulic  fracturing  involves  injecting  high-pressure  fluid  into  rock  formations  to 

release  trapped  hydrocarbons.  It  has  been  used since the 1940s but was significantly 

improved  in  the  early  2000s.  By  2016,  hydraulically  fractured  horizontal  wells 

accounted  for  69%  of  all  oil  and  natural  gas  wells  drilled  in  the  United  States  and 

83%23  of  the  total  linear  footage  drilled  (EIA,  2018). This new abundance of energy 

22 Independent Petroleum Association of America. (n.d.). Hydraulic fracturing: Unlocking America’s energy resources. 
https://www.ipaa.org/fracking/  
23  U.S.  Energy  Information  Administration.  (2018,  January  30).  Hydraulically  fractured  horizontal  wells  account  for 
most new oil and natural gas wells. https://www.eia.gov/todayinenergy/detail.php?id=34732  

 
35 
allowed the U.S. to switch from a net importer to a major exporter of energy.  In 2019, 

U.S. energy exports surpassed imports for the first time in over six decades24. In 2023 

total  energy  exports  reached  a  record  high  of  approximately 29.50 quads, exceeding 

imports  by  about  7.80  quads25.  Favourable  market  conditions  also contributed to the 

success of the USA energy production. High oil prices in the mid-2000s incentivized 

investment  in  unconventional  resources.  Increase  in  prices  was  driven  partially  by 

rapidly  developing  Chinese  industries.  Between  2000  and  2007,  China’s oil demand 

grew  by  nearly  7%  per  year,  putting  pressure  on  supply  (IEEJ,  2009). In July 2008, 

the  nominal  price  of  oil  peaked  at  $145  per  barrel  (Hamilton,  2009).  This  increase 

made  previously  uneconomical  oil  extraction  methods,  such  as  unconventional  oil 

development  (e.g.  hydraulic  fracturing),  financially  viable.  Moreover,  unlike  many 

countries where subsurface resources are state-owned, U.S. landowners have the right 

to  profit  from  the  minerals beneath their land (U.S. Department of the Interior, n.d.). 

This  encouraged  widespread  exploration  and  development.  The  rise  in  domestic 

production  had  profound  implications  for  U.S.  oil  imports.  As  local  output  grew, 

reliance  on  foreign oil diminished, leading to a significant reduction in imports. This 

shift  enhanced  energy  security  and  altered  trade  balances.  The  U.S.  also  began 

exporting  more  petroleum  products,  capitalizing  on  its  refining  capacity  and  the 

quality of its crude oil. Political shifts also contributed to increased interest in energy 

production.  The  beginning  of  the Barack Obama administration in 2009 was marked 

by  attention  to  energy  security.  He  stated:  “We  have  known  for  decades  that  our 

survival depends on finding new sources of energy. Yet we import more oil today than 

ever  before”26  (The  White  House,  2009).  Secretary  Vilsack  later  proclaimed: 

“Expanding  our  biofuels  infrastructure  provides  a  unique  opportunity  to  spur  rural 

24 USAFacts. (2023, May 14). Is the US energy independent? https://usafacts.org/articles/is-the-us-energy-independent/  
25  U.S.  Energy  Information  Administration.  (2024,  July  15).  U.S.  energy  facts  explained:  Imports  and  exports. 
https://www.eia.gov/energyexplained/us-energy-facts/imports-and-exports.php  
26  The  White  House.  (2009,  July  6).  Transcript  of  President  Obama's 
https://obamawhitehouse.archives.gov/the-press-office/transcript-president-obamas-interview-with-novaya-gazeta  

interview  with  Novaya  Gazeta. 

 
36 
economic  development  while  reducing  our  dependence  on  foreign  oil  -  one  of  the 

great challenges of the 21st century”27 (The White House, 2009).  

Thus,  from  a  neoclassical  realism  perspective,  the  American  shale  revolution 

can  be  regarded  as  a  strategic  response  to  pressures  in  the  international  system. 

Neoclassical  realists  argue  that  states  respond  to  external  opportunities  and  threats 

mediated  by  domestic  variables  like  political  leadership,  technological  innovation, 

and  institutional  arrangements  (Ripsman,  Taliaferro,  &  Lobell,  2009).  The  rapid 

increase  in  U.S.  energy  production  since  2008  was  more  than  an  economic 

phenomenon  but  a  deliberate  rebalancing  to  enhance  national  power  and  reduce 

strategic  vulnerability  to  oil  supply  shocks.  Soaring  oil  prices, political instability in 

the  historic  source  regions,  and  growing  global  competition  —  particularly  from  a 

rising  China  —  created  incentives  for  the  U.S.  to  be  more  energy  self-sufficient. 

Domestic technological innovation and political priority on energy security, driven by 

leadership under both George W. Bush and Barack Obama, allowed the United States 

to react to these systemic incentives. Dueck (2009) via Ripsman, Taliaferro, & Lobell, 

tested  his  model  on  three  interventions  conducted  by  the  USA  (Iraq,  Vietnam  and 

Korea)  and  showed  that  initiation  was  due  to  external  threat,  but  execution  and 

political  framing  was  deeply  conditioned  by  domestic  politics,  which  is  a  classical 

situation.  The  presidents  of  the  USA  tend  to  initiate  major  interventions  due  to 

internal  beliefs  on  national interests. This is also discussed in foreign policy analysis 

theories, which claim bounded rationality and absence of full knowledge to be reasons 

for  certain  foreign  policy  decisions  (Simon,  1957).  Domestic  factors  are  very 

important  for  justification  of  foreign  actions.  Presidents  in  the  U.S.  have  significant 

autonomy  in  foreign  policy,  especially  regarding  security,  but  they  still  have  to 

navigate  public  opinion, party politics and media narratives, which also contribute to 

justification.  Thus,  apart  from  second  and 

third  ‘images’, 

individual  level 

27  The  White  House.  (2009,  May  5).  President  Obama  announces  steps  to  support  sustainable  energy  options. 
https://obamawhitehouse.archives.gov/the-press-office/president-obama-announces-steps-support-sustainable-energy-opt
ions  

 
37 
decision-making  matters  as  well.  But  this  will  be  discussed  more  in  constructivist 

explanation.  Domestic  politics  do  not  fully  cause  foreign  actions  but  influence  their 

form  and  how  they are conducted. In the U.S. presidents consult their perceptions of 

national  interest  when  thinking  about  aggressive  foreign  policies  (Dueck,  2009). 

Thus,  seeing  national  interest  in  defining  the  global energy market for U.S. interests 

and deteriorating EU-Russia relations led to increased perception of Russia as a threat 

and  subsequent  attempt  to  convince  key  Russian  partners  in  that.  The  U.S. 

administrations  used  both  hard  and  soft  power  in  line  with  national  interest 

conceptions but these were framed differently under Obama, Trump and Biden. Thus, 

the  shale  revolution  can  be  described  as  a  case  where  material  capabilities  (energy 

production)  were  increased  to enforce the U.S.’s relative position in the international 

system.  

 
 
2.2 Securitization of Russian Energy in the U.S. and the EU  

38 

Constructivist theory of IR is an important tool to analyze behavior, actions and 

motivations  of  the  U.S.,  Russia  and  the  EU  to  reveal  the  link  of  construction  of 

identity,  framing  of  threats  and  subsequent  measures taken by states. Constructivism 

as  a  theory  in  IR  was  developed  in  the  1990s.  It questions materialism and ascribed 

rationalism of actors in previous theories and focuses on the role of ideas, maintaining 

that  international  politics  is  shaped  not  only  by  material  power  but  also  by  social 

structures,  shared  ideas,  and  intersubjective  meanings.  It  considers  states  acting  in 

international  anarchy,  similar  to  realism and liberalism, but this anarchy is not stable 

and objective, rather constructed by states themselves, reacting to contexts depending 

on  their  perception of reality. According to Guzzini (2000), main constructivist ideas 

are  social  construction  of  knowledge  and  of  social  reality.  Alexander  Wendt,  one of 

the  first  constructivist scholars, famously claimed in his foundational work ‘Anarchy 

is What States Make of It: The Social Construction of Power Politics’ (1992) that all 

aspects  of  relations  between  states  are  products  of  human  action  that  historically 

constructed identities and norms, including threats, national interest, balance of power 

and  importance  given  to  material  structures  and  capabilities.  Multiple  meanings  can 

be assigned to different ideas. Constructivism rejects objectivity of systemic pressures 

and  justification  of  foreign  policy actions through objective reality reasoning. Wendt 

(1987)  discussed  agent-structure  debate,  pointing  to  the  fact  that  agent and structure 

are  mutually  constitutive  —  the  international  system  influences  states  in  the  same 

manner that states are able to influence the system. States are also influenced by each 

other, and are able to regulate and leverage this influence.  

In Social Theory of International Politics, Wendt (1999) observed three cultures 

of  anarchy.  Different  representations  of  Self  and  Other  influence  these  cultures. 

Identity  and  national  interest  are  linked.  If  Russia  perceives  itself  as  a  large  energy 

exporter and important partner of the European Union — its interest is to maintain it. 

It is similar to the ‘otherness’ applied by Western culture to Eastern, especially given 

 
39 

ideological 

rivalry  between  Russian 

traditional  values  and  U.S-promoted 

progressivism. Russia-EU relations in the 1990s were based on friendship and mutual 

desire  for  cooperation  —  Kantian  culture,  where  partnership  and  diplomacy  are 

prioritised;  then,  between  2006  and 2014, Lockean culture came — characterized by 

suspicion  and  competition,  subsequently  leading  to  advent  of  Hobbesian  culture 

which  prevails  nowadays  —  with  no  trust  and  desire  to  rely  on  each  other.  In 

U.S.-Russia  relations,  truly  Kantian  friendship,  was  not  clearly  present.  It  would  be 

more  correct  to  say  that  these  relations  began  from  Lockean  rivalry  leading  to 

Hobbesian  in  the 2000s which preserves nowadays in an explicit form by both parts. 

These  ‘cultures’  are  not  determined  by  structure  of  reality, as in Waltz’s neorealism, 

rather, by historically contingent social practices and shared understandings that shape 

how states behave nowadays.  

Values  are  very  important  for  construction  of national interest. Constructivism 

supposes  that  states  with  radically different identities behave as realists — aiming to 

gain  power  and  prevent  changing  balance  while  maintaining  survival  through 

self-help strategies, while those with similar identities behave as liberals — aiming to 

preserve  peace  by  cooperation,  negotiation  and  economic  integration.  This  confirms 

that  the  feeling  of otherness contributes to subjective perception of states and threats 

from  it  —  contributing  to  rivalry  between  Russia  and the USA, where the European 

Union  appeared to be an involved actor, which also has domestic interests, but is not 

fully  independent  in  the  formulation  of  a  greater  agenda  towards  Russia.  For 

constructivists  threats  are  not  given,  but  issues  are  framed  as  security  concerns 

through  discourse  and  institutional  practices.  Russia-EU  economic  relations  are 

represented not purely by rational utility maximising actions, but are deeply political, 

and from 2014 till 2022 were strongly constructed as a security threat by the EU itself, 

and by external actors with their national interests, such as the USA.  

Securitisation  theory  of  the  Copenhagen  school  was  developed  by  a  group  of 

scholars.  Foundational  work  of  Buzan,  Wæver  &  de  Wilde  (1998)  highlights  how 

 
40 
threats  are  becoming  objectivized  and  given extreme importance as security matters. 

In  contrast  to  traditional  security  studies,  in  the securitization framework it could be 

said  that  ‘threats  are  what  states  make  of them’ — as everything can become a vital 

threat  if  correctly  framed.  Securitization  shows  how the language of security is used 

to  emphasize  certain  issues  as  more  important  and  dangerous  than  others.  These 

securitising moves — arguments promoted through language, diffusion of the idea of 

being  threatened  by,  for  example,  dependence  on  Russian  gas  —  if  accepted  by 

decision-making  elites,  provoke  securitising extraordinary measures that are justified 

as pursuit of national interest and defence of the state and people. This helps the state 

to  mobilise  public  support.  The  USA  by  promoting  the  idea  of  Russian  threat, 

supported  and  reinforced  by  the  EU,  contributed  to  break  of  the  partnership  and 

increase of rivalry.  

Securitization  is  an  important  part  of  modern  wars  and  other  questionable 

decisions  that  are  generally  not  supported  by  the  public  —  with  globalization  of 

information  it  became  crucial  to  justify  actions  against  the  masses  (especially  in 

democracies  —  as  the  USA  and  EU  states).  Thus,  the  influence  of  the  media  and 

articulated  discourse  plays  a role. By shaping perceptions of Russian gas as coercive 

and  politically  dangerous  (securitizing  moves),  the  U.S.  contributed  to  EU’s 

securitization of energy dependency (acceptance of the move) and to delegitimization 

of  Russia  as  a  reliable  supplier  —  with  radical  actions  and  rejection  of  cooperation 

(extraordinary  measures).  These  measures  do  not  have to be rational (as classical IR 

theories  assume),  but  can  be  results  of  miscalculations  and  subjectivity.  Internal 

concerns  over  Russian  gas  dangers  were  present  in  the  EU  since  the  2006  crisis, 

reinforced  in  2009  —  just  the  time  of  the  shale  revolution,  but  significant  actions 

began  after  2014,  a  very  convenient  moment  for  the  USA  to  contribute  to  the 

discourse that blames Russia and to rapidly increase LNG exports. At the same time, 

the  EU’s  response  was  shaped  not  only  by  threat  narratives  but  also  by  market 

competition and asymmetric interdependencies.  

 
2.3 EU-Russia Energy Relations Before 2022 

41 

Neoliberal  institutionalism  and  complex interdependence theories are useful to 

explain  EU-Russia  relations  before  2022.  These  theories,  developed  by  Robert 

Keohane  and  Joseph  Nye,  emphasize  that  states  cooperate  not because they are nice 

but  because  institutions,  regimes,  and  economic  interdependence  create  mutual 

benefit,  predictability,  and  constraint  of  unilateral  action  (Keohane  &  Nye,  1977; 

Keohane,  1984).  There  are  multiple  channels  and actors involved and foreign policy 

of state is influenced by: goals of actors (EU institutions, state interests, corporations, 

Russian  energy companies and authorities etc.); instruments of state policy — ability 

to manipulate interdependence to promote national agendas — what Russia did using 

energy  as  a  "tool  of  intimidation  and  blackmail"28  (words of   Dick Cheney, the Vice 

President  of  the  USA)  (Bigg,  2006)  —  this  was  the  case  due  to  asymmetric 

interdependence  in  which  Russia  had  opportunity  to  be  better  off  and  prioritise 

political  gains  over  economic;  and  agenda  formation  —  multiplicity  of  actors  gives 

opportunity  to  all  of  them  promote  their  agenda  and  justify  actions  through  it. 

Neoliberal scholars claim that high levels of interconnectedness, especially economic, 

mitigate  conditions  of  anarchy  and  prevent  conflicts  (Nye  and  Keohane,  2012).  But 

this cannot fully explain events in EU-Russia relations after 2022, when actors ceased 

to value economic priorities over ideological and strategic ones. Events after the 2022 

Ukraine crisis may seem irrational from liberal point of view: these actions prevented 

friendly  mutually  beneficial  development  of  geographically  close  economies  of 

Russia, Ukraine and European states for years. Further actions and relations are better 

explained  by  constructivist  securitization  of  energy  dependence,  which  goes back to 

2000s  crises  and  suspicions  supported  by  the  USA.  Overall,  in  conditions  of 

interdependence states value absolute gains and prioritise cooperation and friendship. 

That once was the case of Russia and the EU. 

28  Bigg,  C. 
RadioFreeEurope/RadioLiberty. https://www.rferl.org/a/1068299.html  

(2006,  May  10).  Russia:  Putin  speech  puts  domestic  concerns  before 

foreign  policy. 

 
42 

Russia  for  many  years  defined  the  European  Union  as  a  valuable  partner  and 

saw  broad  perspectives  in  the  development  of  cooperation  and  shared  projects. 

Establishment  of  political  partnership,  free  access  to  the  EU  market  and  financial 

assistance were priority interests in relations with the EU in the 1990s. This is evident 

in the adoption of the EU-Russia partnership and cooperation agreement (1994)29 and 

in  the  overall  Kozyrev  doctrine  that  defined  Russia  as  a  part  of  Western  world. 

European  identity  was  desirable  and  efforts  of  economic  integration,  cultural 

exchange  and  political  relations  were  promoted.  This  desire  was  mutual  and  was 

reciprocally  reinforced  since  the  1990s.  Cooperation  began  in  Soviet  times,  when 

there was no Russian Federation, nor the European Union, but were their predecessors 

in  the  face  of  the  USSR  and  the  European  Communities.  Institutionalized  and 

accepted rules and market benefits were the basis for this partnership. Historically, the 

EU  and  Russia  have maintained significant energy ties, with Russia being one of the 

EU’s largest suppliers of natural gas and oil.  

In  the  majority  of  the  post-Cold  War  period,  energy  relations  between  Russia 

and  the  EU  were  structured  by  the  logic  of  Russia  being  a  reliable  supplier  and 

European  market  being  lucrative  for  the  Russian  budget,  resembling  the  logic  of 

neoliberal  institutionalism.  This  relationship  has  been  mutually beneficial, but led to 

mutual dependence. The dependency rate was equal to 56% in 200030, and to 58% in 

2018,  with  Russia  still  standing  on  the  first  place  as  the  import  state  of  crude  oil, 

natural  gas  and  fossil  fuels  (Eurostat, 2020). However, these ties have also produced 

geopolitical  tension,  particularly  as  the  EU  seeks  to  balance  its energy security with 

political,  environmental  and  strategic  considerations.  Despite  deep  economic 

interdependence,  internal  disagreements  in  the  EU  and  asymmetrical  dependence 

produced concerns and contradictions over the relationship with Russia. 

While the European Union is commonly regarded as acting as a single actor in

the  field  of  energy  policy,  member  states did not have a unique view on dependence 

on Russian gas. Constructivism can explain this divergence, as identity of state and its 

perceptions  of  external  environment  are influenced by historical ties and perceptions 

of past experience. For instance, Germany’s cooperative energy policy was shaped by 

its  strong  industrial  ties  to  Russia  and  its  Ostpolitik  heritage,  which  saw  long-term 

agreements  and  projects  like  Nord  Stream  as  stabilizers  rather  than  threats.  In 

contrast,  Poland  and  the  Baltic  States,  based  on  historical  experience  of  Soviet 

hegemony,  viewed  Russian  energy  as  a  tool  of  coercion  and  actively  promoted 

diversification  and  EU-level  regulation.  This  divergence  was  underpinned by deeper 

national  interests:  while  Western member states appreciated economic efficiency and 

energy  supply  security,  Eastern  members  appreciated  sovereignty  and  threat 

mitigation.  The  European  Commission  increasingly  joined  the  securitization  camp, 

portraying  energy  dependence  as  a  strategic weakness. Meanwhile, nations like Italy 

and Hungary continued to push for closer ties with Russian producers, demonstrating 

that  EU-level  responses  to  Russia’s  energy  policy  were  shaped  by  varied  domestic 

political  calculations  and  elite  threat  perception.  This  internal  fragmentation  slowed 

down  cohesive  energy  policy  and  made  the  EU’s  diversification  away from Russian 

gas  a  controversial  and  slow  process,  only  accelerated  by  external  shocks  like  the 

2014 and 2022 Ukrainian crises. 

Since  2014,  the  geopolitical  landscape  has  been  dramatically  altered  by 

Russia’s  reunification  with  Crimea  and  the  subsequent  conflict  in  Eastern  Ukraine, 

which  in  2022  evolved  into  open  military  confrontation  with  Russia  declaring  a 

beginning  of  a  Special  Military  Operation.  Ukraine  has  always  been  a  ‘geopolitical 

pivot’  (Brzezinski,  1997)  and  a  transit  country  for  Russian  supplies to the EU. With 

growing  instability  in  Ukraine,  2004  Orange  revolution,  2006  and  2009  gas  crises, 

European confidence in reliable supplies through Ukraine slowly downgraded. These 

developments have led to a reassessment of energy dependencies within the EU, with 

growing  concerns  over  Russia’s  willingness to use energy as a geopolitical tool. The 

 
44 
United  States,  recognizing  these  vulnerabilities,  has  actively  sought  to  influence EU 

energy policies, promoting diversification of energy sources and reducing reliance on 

Russian energy. This included diplomatic efforts, economic sanctions, and support for 

alternative  energy  projects,  such as LNG exports from the U.S. and the development 

of  the  Southern  Gas  Corridor.  For  example,  in  2022  the  EU-U.S.  Task  Force  on 

Energy  Security  was established with a specific primary aim outlined: “to reduce the 

EU’s  reliance  on  Russian  energy”31  —  so  the  U.S.  aims  to  directly  help  the  EU  to 

lower  cooperation  with  Russia.  After  2014  EU  neoliberal  institutions  shifted  from 

facilitating  interdependence  with  Russia  to  crafting  resilience  strategies.  This 

comprised  support  for  construction  of  LNG  terminals  in  Poland,  Lithuania,  and 

Croatia,  for  interconnector  pipelines  (such  as  the  Balticconnector  and  BRUA),  and 

serious  investment  in  renewables  infrastructure  through  initiatives  like the Recovery 

and  Resilience  Facility32.  These  moves  represent  a  functional  deployment  of 

institutional  capacity  not  towards  cooperation, but towards market reorientation. The 

EU  Energy  Union  plan  (2015)33  and  REPowerEU  plan  (2022)34  also  had  a 

well-defined  normative  and  strategic aim in reshaping the EU’s energy policy. These 

plans redefined energy dependence as a vulnerability that must be actively addressed. 

Security of supply, diversification, and internal market integration were foregrounded 

in  the  Energy  Union,  which  was  created  as  a  response  to  the  2014  Crimean  crisis. 

REPowerEU, created in response to the 2022 instability in Ukraine, had the particular 

aim of ending EU dependence on Russian fossil fuels, with LNG imports, renewables, 

and upgrading infrastructure being prioritized. It is stated there that this is: “a plan to 

rapidly  reduce  dependence  on  Russian  fossil  fuels  and  fast  forward  the  green

energy and reset the strategic direction of the EU.  

In  general,  the  initial  basis  of  EU-Russia  energy  ties  was  interdependence, 

which  reflected  reciprocal  economic  gain  and  formalized  engagement  under  shared 

regulations. However, internal contradictions in EU states’ threat perceptions, national 

interests,  elite  narratives,  and  external  geopolitical  crises,  replaced  the  basis  of 

friendship and cooperation on suspicion and hostility. As a result, the EU increasingly 

reframed energy relations with Russia as an area of strategic burden and put political 

threat  perceptions  as  more  important  ones.  Neoliberal  institutional  tools,  initially 

devised to foster integration and efficiency, were re-routed to enhance diversification.  

A  multi-theory  framework is useful to explain the development of U.S. energy 

policy  and  EU-Russia  energy  relations  from  2008  to  2024.  Neoclassical  realism 

explains  how  America  used  its  shale  revolution  to  gain  more  strategic  autonomy  as 

well  as  projecting  power  abroad,  particularly in Europe. Constructivist securitization 

theory explains how discourse in both the EU and the USA built Russian energy as an 

existential  threat, thus warranting extraordinary measures. Neoliberal institutionalism 

sheds  light on the economic interdependence which earlier bound Russia and the EU 

together but then broke apart due to asymmetric dependencies. Acting in tandem, the 

U.S.  was  an  actor  that  not  only  played  a  part  in  material  alterations  (such  as  LNG 

exports)  but  also  helped  to  shape  the  ideational  sphere  which  drove  the  EU  to 

conceptualize energy from an economic to a security issue. This complex dynamic of 

material power, discourse, and institutional logic gave rise to a gradual but deep shift 

in the energy architecture and strategic position of Europe.

CHAPTER 3: ANALYSIS OF THE U.S. ENERGY POLICY AND ITS 
GLOBAL IMPACT (2008-2024)  

The  emergence  of  the  United  States  as  a  top  energy  producer  since  2008 

transformed  the  global  energy  map.  The  shale  revolution  made  it  possible  for 

succeeding  U.S.  administrations  to  pursue  an  ambitious  energy  policy  that  included 

using  energy  as  a  strategic  tool  for  foreign  policy,  reshaping  global  energy  patterns, 

gaining access to foreign markets for U.S. liquefied natural gas, and achieving energy 

independence.  This  chapter  examines  the  evolution  of  U.S.  global  energy  policy 

between 2008 and 2024 in four broad phases: 2008-2013 (from the shale revolution to 

Crimean  crisis),  2014-2016  (from  Crimean  crisis  to  Trump  administration), 

2017-2021 

(Trump 

administration 

and  Biden 

administration  until  2022 

Russia-Ukraine  conflict),  and  2022-2024  (from  Russia-Ukraine  conflict  to  recent 

times),  examining  the  ways  in  which  Washington  used  economic,  normative, 

diplomatic,  and  discursive  tools  to  enhance  its  position  in  the  world  energy  system. 

Although  specific  focus  is  given  to  the  implications  for  Russia  and  EU-Russia 

relations, the analysis places these events in the larger context of U.S. efforts to open 

up  energy  market  access,  counter  competitors,  and  advance  American  energy  as  an 

economic resource and a geopolitical tool.  

3.1 2008-2013: Shale Revolution and Multilateral Engagement 

Between  2008  and  2014,  the  United  States  began  its  strategic  re-entry  into 

global  energy  markets,  driven  primarily  by  the  shale  revolution.  Advances  in 

technology  led  to  growth  in  oil  and  gas  production  and  paved  the  way  for  future 

energy exports. According to the IEA (2019), the shale revolution allowed the U.S. to 

significantly transform energy markets both at home and abroad, shifting from energy 

scarcity  to  abundance,  boosting  global  energy  security  and  thus  becoming  a  global 

leader  in  energy  technologies  and  altering  trade  flows  particularly  via  crude  oil  and 

 
47 
LNG exports. Dr Fatih Birol, the IEA’s Executive Director, supported36 U.S. efforts to 

simplify regulatory approval process for LNG exports as a benefit for importer states’ 

diversification of supplies. Overall, the U.S. was very successful in benefiting from its 

energy: it became the world’s largest producer of crude oil in 2018, surpassing Saudi 

Arabia, the largest exporter of liquefied natural gas in 2022, surpassing Australia and 

Qatar37 and a net total energy exporter in 2019 (exported more energy than imported) 

for  the  first  time  since  the  1950s38.  Between  2008  and  2024  U.S.  leadership  was 

market-driven,  with  increased  interest  in  times  of  better  ability  to  promote  national 

interest and benefit from new markets. It involved institutional infrastructure, like the 

deregulation  of  export  controls,  authorization  for  the  first  LNG  terminals,  and 

regulatory  reforms  by  the  DOE  and  FERC.  The  U.S.  framed  its  energy transition in 

the  context  of  energy  independence  and  technological  superiority,  providing  itself  a 

stable influential role on the world stage in the years ahead.  

In  2008,  U.S.  foreign  policy  reflected  a  period  of  cooperation,  pragmatic 

engagement,  and  strategic  energy  diplomacy.  There  were  22  official  U.S.  press 

releases  related  to  Russia  that  year.  In  general,  relations  were peaceful and aimed at 

mutual  respect  and  desire  to  preserve  neutrality.  High-level  diplomatic  engagement 

included the visit of Under Secretary Reuben Jeffery III to Moscow for economic and 

energy  talks39,  and  the  launch  of  the  U.S.-Russia  Economic  Dialogue  under  the

broader  Strategic  Framework  Declaration40,  aimed  at  deepening  institutionalized

Civil  nuclear  energy  cooperation  was  a  central  pillar  of  U.S.  engagement. 

Several  major  agreements  were  signed:  with  Bahrain41,  the  UAE42,  India43,  and

and  Nonproliferation  reemphasized  shared  vision  for  expansion  of  civilian  nuclear

the 

developing  nations45.  Beyond  nuclear  policy,  the  U.S.  strengthened  its  role  in 

renewable  and  alternative  energy  markets.  It  showcased  climate  leadership  at  the 

Washington  International  Renewable  Energy  Conference46  and  promoted  sustainable 

development through biofuels cooperation with Brazil47 and third countries in Africa, 

Latin  America48,  and  the  Caribbean.  It  also  supported  energy  efficiency  in  China 

through the Asia-Pacific Partnership49. 

However,  the  tone  of  U.S.  discourse  toward  Russia  began  to  shift  after  the 

August  2008  Russia-Georgia  war.  Washington’s  response  portrayed  Russia  as  a

destabilizing  actor  in  the  post-Soviet  space,  calling  for  international  monitors50  and 

emphasizing  Georgia’s  territorial  integrity51  —  laying  early groundwork for the later 

securitization  of  Russian  behavior  and  energy  policy.  Overall,  U.S.  global  energy 

policy in 2008 was defined by cooperative engagement, institution-building in nuclear 

governance,  market-based  expansion  into  alternative  fuels,  and  early  signs  of 

geopolitical reorientation in response to Russian actions. 

In 2009 there were a total 11 press releases that directly or indirectly referenced 

Russia  within  the  context  of  energy  diplomacy  and  overall  25  releases  related  to 

energy.  They  referred  to  high-level  engagements,  multilateral  summits,  bilateral 

cooperation,  strategic  communication  on  global  energy  security  and  climate 

diplomacy.  The  United  States  sought  to  reassert  global  leadership  not  only  through 

military  alliances  or  market  dominance,  but  by  shaping  norms  and  institutional 

frameworks  around  energy  security  and  climate  governance.  The  Obama 

administration  supported  constructive  dialogue  with  Russia  through  the  U.S.-Russia 

Bilateral  Presidential  Commission,  where  energy  was  one  of  the  central  areas  of 

mutual  interest,  framed  as  requiring  transparency  and  market  liberalization.  This 

Commission  included  Energy  and  Environment  and  Nuclear  Energy  and  Nuclear 

Security  working  groups  (The  White  House,  2009).  In  general,  the  beginning of the 

Obama presidency coincided with hopes for liberal turn of Russia under Medvedev’s. 

Russia  suffered  from  the  2008 economic crisis and needed money for modernization 

of  the  economy,  so  cooperation  with  the  West  was  a  priority,  while  non-Western 

partners  were  still  very  important  for  economic  and  image  development  on  the 

international  arena.  NATO  enlargement  was  freezed  for  a  time  and  in  general  there 

was  less  criticism  of  Russia  under  the  ‘reset’.  A  North  Distribution  Network  was 

established  that  allowed  the  USA  to  supply  NATO  in  Afghanistan  through  Russia.

The NATO-Russia council began to function again after its suspension in 2008 due to 

Russian  actions  in  Georgia.  Thus,  in  general, there were mutual hopes on increasing 

trust, transparency and economic cooperation. Bilateral energy diplomacy with Russia 

— energy cooperation in the U.S.-Russia ‘reset’, discursive shift marked by Secretary 

Clinton’s  repeated  emphasis52  on  mutual  respect,  shared  responsibility,  pragmatic 

engagement  and  joint  technological  collaboration  in  areas like energy efficiency and 

Arctic policy signaled a move from containment to cooperative security — especially 

tempered  by  concerns  over  Iran,  Georgia,  and  NATO  expansion.  After  the  2009 

Ukraine-Russia  gas  crisis  the  USA  supported  diversification  of  European  gas 

supplies:  “...  need  for  transparent,  market-oriented  arrangements  for  the  sale  and 

shipment  of  natural  gas  and  the  importance  of  diversifying  energy  supplies.  The 

United  States  stands  ready  to  support  European  Union  efforts  to  enhance  its  energy 

security.”  —  said  Robert  Wood53,  acting  State  Department  spokesman.  Despite 

general  attempts  to  build  positive  relations,  U.S.  actions  began  to  support  European 

desire to avoid Russian supplies. Important element was positioning climate change as 

a core issue.  

The U.S. promoted itself as a leader in the climate change fight. In April 2009 it 

launched  the  Major  Economies  Forum  on  Energy  and  Climate,  framing  climate 

cooperation  as  urgent  and  central  to  global  stability,  formulating  securitization  of 

climate  change  as  a  shared  global  security  concern,  allowing  the  U.S.  to  convene 

major economies as co-stakeholders in the transition to low-carbon development (U.S. 

Department of State, 2009). In the Asia-Pacific, the Obama administration promoted a 

shared  leadership  model,  particularly  in  the  U.S.-China  energy  diplomacy.  The  July 

2009 Memorandum of Understanding on Enhancing Cooperation on Climate Change,

The  launch  of  the  Bilateral  Energy  Security  Working  Group  with  Ukraine57

assistance)  and  the  Pakistan  Signature  Energy  Program58  (with  $125  million  help) 

exemplified  energy  diplomacy  as  soft  power  —  addressing  development  challenges 

while  advancing  U.S.  geopolitical  interests.  By  framing  energy  aid  in  terms  of 

infrastructure  modernization,  governance  reform,  and  private sector engagement, the 

U.S. projected itself as a stabilizing force in Eurasia and South Asia.  

Discourse  around  nuclear  energy  further  reveals  how  the  U.S.  leveraged  legal 

and normative frameworks to counter proliferation threats. The 2009 U.S.-UAE “123 

Agreement”59  was  celebrated  as  a  model  of  peaceful  nuclear  development  —

advocated  energy  and  climate  goals  and  redefined  international  energy  politics 

terminology.  The  U.S. became a global norm entrepreneur, bridging the gap between 

developed  and  developing  countries,  security  and  sustainability,  and  rivalry  and 

cooperation.  By  placing  energy  within  the  diplomacy vocabulary — clean, peaceful, 

verifiable,  shared  —  the  U.S.  set  the  cognitive  and  institutional  structure  of 

international energy management in 2009. 

In  2010  there  were  26  press  releases  regarding  energy  governance  and 

cooperation.  The  Energy  and  Climate  Partnership  of  the  Americas  (ECPA)  was  a 

cornerstone  of  U.S.  energy  diplomacy  in  Latin  America.  U.S.  officials  promoted  a 

governance  framework  built  on  five  pillars:  efficiency,  renewables,  fossil  fuel 

transition,  infrastructure,  and  energy  access.  These  efforts  were  reinforced  at  the 

World Urban Forum60 in March, where Brazil’s sustainable urbanization initiative was 

praised and supported through the U.S.-backed academic and policy networks. At the 

ECPA  Ministerial61  in  April,  Secretary  Clinton  launched  new  initiatives  including 

biomass  energy  research,  Caribbean  interconnection  dialogues,  and  Peace  Corps 

deployments for grassroots clean energy access. This broadened ECPA’s reach beyond 

mitigation into adaptation and urban development. An accompanying joint op-ed62 by 

Secretaries  Hillary  Clinton  and  Steven  Chu  framed  clean  energy  cooperation  as  a 

strategic  engine  for  hemispheric  prosperity.  On  the  global  stage,  the  U.S.  advanced 

dual-track  climate  diplomacy  —  supporting 

the  UNFCCC  while  shaping

through  the  lens  of  fissile  material  management.  Further,  NATO-Russia  Council 

talks71  in  September  revealed  European  security  concerns,  underlining that Russia is 

seen  as  a  partner  and  not  an  adversary  in  maintaining  security  in  Europe.  In  the 

U.S.-EU  Energy  Council72 in November, energy diplomacy centered on the Southern 

Corridor, Ukrainian market reform, and shared research on smart grids, CCS, and rare 

earths.  The  Council  stressed  transatlantic  regulatory  convergence,  especially  in 

offshore drilling and unconventional gas development, showing institutional maturity 

in balancing market innovation with environmental oversight.  

A defining element of 2010 policy was the escalation of energy-linked pressure 

on  Iran.  Through  diplomatic  outreach  and  legal  risk  signaling,  the  U.S.  convinced73 

major firms like Shell, Total, LUKOIL, and Trafigura to withdraw from Iran’s energy 

sector.  These  actions  demonstrated  how  energy  diplomacy  could  be  used  to  isolate 

adversaries, enforce non-proliferation norms, and align market behavior with security 

objectives. Meanwhile, the U.S.-Canada Energy Consultative Mechanism74 reaffirmed 

North  America’s  integrated  approach  to  energy  governance,  focusing  on  offshore 

regulation,  CCS,  and  renewables.  In  2010,  U.S.  foreign  energy  policy  was 

characterized  by  institutionalization,  innovation,  and  integration. Whether deepening 

ties  with  allies,  responding  to  crises,  isolating  adversaries,  or  steering  global norms, 

the  Obama  administration  embedded  energy  into  its  broader  strategy  of  multilateral 

leadership  and  economic  diplomacy. From shale diplomacy and clean energy forums 

to nuclear security and market exits from Iran, energy became a strategic multiplier — 

blending climate imperatives with geopolitical calculus and statecraft.

In 2011, American foreign energy policy was marked by a shift toward making 

energy  diplomacy  a  pillar  of  national  security,  leadership  on  climate  change,  and 

geopolitics.  There  were  27  press  releases  on  energy  and  Russia.  With  rhetoric 

emphasizing transparency, innovation, and rule-of-law-driven governance, the United 

States positioned itself as a normative leader in creating international energy markets 

— often implicitly contrasted to Russia's state-directed and fossil-fuel-ordered model. 

A  defining  innovation  was  the  establishment  of  the  Bureau  of  Energy  Resources, 

launched  to  institutionalize  energy  as  a  core  component  of  U.S.  diplomacy. 

Ambassador Carlos Pascual75 established its mandate on three pillars: the handling of 

energy  geopolitics,  the  promotion  of  clean  technology transitions, and the expansion 

of global energy access. Notably, Pascual’s references to Russia were depoliticized — 

framings of as a ‘key producer’ whose market stability cooperation was necessary — 

repeating  a  technocratic,  market-oriented  language  aimed  at  diffusing  geopolitical 

strife  while  reaffirming  control  of  global  gas  flows.  US  involvement  in  multilateral 

venues  such  as  the  International  Renewable  Energy  Agency  (IRENA)  and  the 

U.S.-EU  Energy  Council  also  supported  this  normative agenda. At IRENA, the U.S. 

aligned  with  decarbonization  principles that were not in Russia's hydrocarbon export 

interests.  At  the  Energy  Council  the  U.S.,  while  recognizing  Russia  as  an 

‘indispensable  part  of  Europe's  and  the  world's  energy  solutions,’76  simultaneously 

appeals  to  Moscow  to  adopt  open  reforms  and  be  a  supporter  of  market  openness. 

This dual position — recognizing Russia's energy importance while appealing to it to 

conform  to  Western  standards  —  reflects  the  U.S.  role  in  mediating  the  EU's 

dependence  on  Russian  hydrocarbons.  Moreover,  U.S.  support  for  projects  like  the 

Southern  Corridor,  Ukrainian  integration  into  European  energy  networks,  and 

investment  promotion  cooperation  subtly  shifts  EU  energy  policy  towards  Russian 

supply  alternatives  (U.S.  Department  of  State,  2011).  Discursively,  America 

75  U.S.  Department  of  State.  (2011,  November  16).  Briefing  on  the  new  Bureau  of  Energy  Resources  within  the 
Department of State (Remarks by Ambassador Carlos Pascual). https://2009-2017.state.gov/e/enr/rls/177281.htm  
76 U.S. Department of State. (2011, November 29). Joint statement on the U.S.-EU Energy Council Ministerial (Media 
Note No. 2011/2018). https://2009-2017.state.gov/r/pa/prs/ps/2011/11/177856.htm  

 
56 
positioned  itself  as  a  standard-setter,  using  regulatory  cooperation  and  innovation 

talks  to  promote  market  liberalization  and  efficiency.  Through  targeted  bilateral 

efforts,  such  as  the  MOUs  with  Poland77  and  Lithuania78,  the  U.S.  extended  its 

presence  in  traditionally  Russia-aligned  regions  through  the  promotion  of  energy 

diversification  and  non-Russian gas alternatives such as ‘unconventional natural gas’ 

(shale  gas  of  the  USA)  and  LNG.  These  ties  were  cast  in  the  terms  of 

‘capacity-building’  and  ‘technical  cooperation’  to  establish  the  legitimacy  of 

American standards while undermining Russia's infrastructural monopolies. 

Sanctions  design,  especially  across  Iran,  exemplified  how  energy  policy  was 

facilitating  greater  strategic  interest  in  containment.  Discourse  surrounding  legal 

compliance and global responsibility justified measures taken by the United States to 

divorce  firms  such  as  Lukoil  from  Iranian  markets,  coercively  targeting  Russia  and 

the  preeminence  of  U.S.-delineated  trade  values79  (continuing  2010  discourse  and 

evading  energy  companies  from  cooperation with Iran). In the South, efforts like the 

Caribbean  Renewable  Energy  Forum80  and  Libyan  oil  sales  assistance81  during  the 

civil war showcased U.S. capacity to break up rival energy networks and emerge as a 

stabilizing  broker.  Emphasis  on  solar,  geothermal,  and regulatory reform served as a 

discursive  counter-narrative  to  fossil-exporting  regimes,  a  progressive  narrative  of 

decentralized, clean energy leadership. Generally, in 2011, U.S. foreign energy policy 

strategically  exerted  hard  power  (sanctions,  legal  weapons),  soft  power  (norms, 

diplomacy  through  innovation),  and  market-reflective  rhetoric  in  a  campaign  of 

reasserted leadership over an evolving world energy order. Russia did not show up as

an  overt  adversary  but  structural competitor — fossil fuel based, centrally led model

In  2012,  out  of  32  releases, 8 related to Russia. The United States employed a 

multifaceted strategy through a combination of sanctions, diplomacy, and institutional 

leadership.  A  major focus of U.S. influence was the isolation of Iran’s energy sector. 

Through  targeted  sanctions  and  the  enforcement  of  Section  1245  of  the  National 

Defense  Authorization  Act  (NDAA),  the  U.S.  incentivized82  key  importers  (such  as 

China,  India,  and  Turkey)  to  reduce  Iranian  oil  purchases,  thus  directly  impacting 

Iran’s  revenue  stream  and  reshaping  global  crude flows toward U.S. allies. All these 

anti-Iran  actions  are  shaped  under  the  issue  of  the  Iranian  nuclear  program.  Similar 

strategies  were  used  to  pressure  corporate  actors  like  Edison  International  to  exit 

Iran’s  energy  market83,  and  to  punish  state-owned  entities  like  Syria’s  Sytrol  for 

engaging  in  Iran-linked  energy  trade84.  The  fourth  U.S.-EU  Energy  Council85 

illustrates  how  the  U.S.  exercised  regulatory and normative leadership by promoting 

transparent  and  secure  energy  markets,  continuing  support 

for  European 

diversification  via  the  Southern  Gas  Corridor,  and  encouraging  the  adoption  of  best 

practices 

in  unconventional  gas  development.  This  aligned  with  a  broader 

transatlantic  effort  to  embed  energy  policy  within  institutional  frameworks  like  the 

IEA, WTO, and EITI, reinforcing a neoliberal institutionalist approach.

Bilateral diplomacy also played a critical role. U.S. agreements with Australia86, 

Georgia87,  and  support  for  Sudan-South  Sudan88  oil  negotiations  reflect  the  use  of 

strategic  partnerships  to  secure  supply  routes,  stabilize  production  regions,  and 

promote U.S.-backed governance norms. At the same time, initiatives like the Energy 

and Climate Partnership of the Americas (ECPA) exemplified soft power tools to fund 

renewable  energy,  efficiency,  and  resilience  projects  in  Latin  America  and  the 

Caribbean89, countering the influence of actors like Venezuela and China. U.S. energy 

diplomacy  also  extended  to  nuclear  governance,  exemplified  by  the  appointment  of 

Ambassador  Joe  Macmanus  to  the  International  Atomic  Energy  Agency  (IAEA)90, 

where  the  U.S.  sought  to  monitor  Iran’s  compliance  and  maintain  leadership  in 

multilateral  non-proliferation  structures.  Overall,  the  U.S.  policies  in  2012  blended 

competition and strategic alliances, norm promotion and institutional rule-making and 

cooperation.  Sanctions,  diplomatic  pressure,  multilateral 

frameworks,  and 

development aid enabled the U.S. to maintain and extend its influence over both fossil 

fuel and emerging energy landscapes. 

In  2013,  the  United  States  adopted  a  multidimensional  strategy,  combining 

coercive  economic  tools,  bilateral  and  multilateral  partnerships,  science  diplomacy, 

and  norm-setting  mechanisms.  Out  of  36  press  releases  regarding  energy,  10  were 

related  to  Russia.  Most  open  U.S.  tool  of  influence  in  2013  remained  its  sanctions 

regime  linked  to  energy  for  Iran  under  Section  1245  of  NDAA  that  were  used  to

Iranian  oil  revenues  as  sponsoring  terrorism.  Such  a  framing  legitimized  the  use  of

shift  from  traditional  resource  diplomacy  to  regulatory  geopolitics.  In  particular, the 

U.S. cooperated very closely with the IAEA to support a verification mechanism that 

would  enshrine  Iran's  compliance  in  international  norms,  further  reinforcing  the 

normative bases of its coercive campaign. 

The U.S.-Russia energy cooperation was robust in 2013, institutionalized in the 

U.S.-Russia  Bilateral  Presidential  Commission  (BPC)94:  it  demonstrated  pragmatic 

cooperation  in  energy-related  science  and  infrastructure  (and  other  issues  such  as 

Syria,  Snowden,  NATO).  The  Energy  Working  Group,  co-chaired  by  U.S.  Secretary 

of  Energy  Ernest  Moniz  and  Russian  Minister  of  Energy  Alexander  Novak,  worked 

on  several  areas: smart grids and electric reliability (e.g., SDG&E collaboration with 

Belgorod’s Helios lighting system), energy modeling and disaster resilience supported 

by  Los  Alamos  National  Laboratory,  a  clean  energy  R&D  portfolio  on 

nanotechnology,  fuel  cells,  hydrogen  storage,  and  biofuels  and  black  carbon

influence  and  interdependence;  second,  to  instill  U.S.  technical  standards  and

deteriorated,  but  the  U.S.  used  subnational  and  scientific  diplomacy to condition the 

trajectory  of  Russian  infrastructure  and  environmental  regulation  development.  But 

this  cooperation  was  coupled  with  strategic  competition,  most  overtly  visible  in 

forums such as the NATO-Russia Council95 and Kerry's repeated bilateral one-on-ones 

with  Lavrov.  In  a  December  2013  briefing,  the  U.S.  pushed  back  against  Lavrov's 

argument that developments in Iran made NATO's missile defense offers irrelevant by 

insisting  again  that  ballistic  missile  systems  (most  commonly  linked  to  WMD 

delivery) remained part of the broader energy-security equation. Thus, while technical 

collaboration  persisted,  strategic  distrust  and  geopolitical  differences  along  regional 

hydrocarbon  conduits  (i.e.,  Syria,  Ukraine)  were  deepening.  The  December 

U.S.-Georgia Strategic Partnership Commission96 reaffirmed Washington's support for 

Georgia's  role  as  a  transit  country  for  Caspian  hydrocarbons  and  its  alignment  with 

EU  energy  security  interests in diversification. A U.S.-Pakistan energy trade mission 

coordinated  U.S.  firm  cooperation  with  Pakistani  oil  and  gas  companies  as  a 

follow-up  to  consultations  under  Working  Groups.  USAID's  installation  of  smart 

meters97  and  upgrading  of  transmission  infrastructure  was  framed  as  one  of  the 

principal  steps  towards  alleviating  the  energy  crisis  in  Pakistan  —  highlighting  not 

only  America's  role  as  donor,  but  technical  standard-bearer  and  system  changer.  In 

Southeast  Asia, the U.S.-Asia Pacific Comprehensive Energy Partnership (USACEP) 

symbolized U.S. efforts to redefine regional energy governance through clean energy

In addition to infrastructure and trade, the U.S. applied climate-coordinated soft 

power  through  energy  influence.  During  the  Greening  Embassies  Forum99,  Kerry 

connected  energy  efficiency  and  U.S.  foreign  policy  identity,  pledging  sustainable 

transformation  of  U.S.  foreign  missions  globally.  Symbolic  alignment  between 

national and foreign energy conduct further solidified the U.S. role as a climate leader 

and  environmental  steward.  Furthermore,  the  wPOWER100  initiative  advocated  for 

gendered  energy  diplomacy,  empowering  over  8,000  women  with  clean  energy 

entrepreneurship  in  East  Africa,  India,  and  Nigeria.  The  program  not only enhanced 

access  to  clean  technologies,  but  it  also  positioned  women  as  agents  of  last-mile 

energy  delivery—inserting  U.S.  power  in  emerging  economies  through  bottom-up, 

community-based  partnerships. 

It's  a  simple  case  of  constructivist  energy 

involvement,  where  identity,  values,  and  empowerment  are  no  less  important  than 

megawatts.  In  2013,  the  United  States  embarked  on  a  strategic  transformation  of 

global  energy  governance  by  acting  at  the  same  time  through  coercive  instruments 

(sanctions  and  trade  policy),  cooperative  institutionalized  frameworks  (e.g.,  BPC, 

USACEP,  ECPA),  and  normative  rhetoric  (transparency,  sustainability,  gender 

equality).

3.2 2014-2016: Crimean Crisis and Increases of LNG Exports 

62 

A  key  characteristic  of  the  2010s  was  the  rise  of  the  U.S.  LNG  exports.  The 

development  of  LNG  export  infrastructure  was  a  crucial  component  of  this 

transformation. There were both economic as well as geopolitical motivations for the 

increase  in  the  U.S.'s  exporting  activity,  since  it  attempted  to  reduce  Europe's 

dependency  on  Russian  gas  following  the  Ukraine  crisis.  The  beginning  of  rapid 

development  in  LNG  production  can  be  attributed  to  Sabine  Pass,  Louisiana  —  the 

first  facility  to  export  liquefied  natural  gas,  with  its  first  shipment  departing  in 

February  2016.  Operated  by  Cheniere  Energy,  the  Sabine  Pass  LNG  terminal  has 

since  expanded  to  include  six  fully  operational  liquefaction  units,  each  capable  of 

producing  approximately  5  million tonnes per annum of LNG. This facility has set a 

precedent for subsequent LNG projects in the U.S (Cheniere Energy, n.d). As a result, 

the  U.S.  LNG  export  capacity  more  than  tripled  since  2018,  with  projections  that  it 

will  nearly  double  by  2030  (IEA,  2024).  The  European Union became a key market 

for U.S. LNG, especially after the 2014 Ukraine crisis, which highlighted the risks of 

over-reliance  on  Russian  gas.  The  U.S.  actively supported European energy supplies 

diversification,  insisting  on  the  aggressive  nature  of  the  Russian  state  and  framing 

Russia  as  an  unreliable  partner  —  securitisation  mechanisms  were  put  at  work  and 

LNG began to be considered a better alternative source of supply, enhancing Europe’s 

energy security. Countries like China, Japan, and South Korea emerged as significant 

buyers  of  U.S.  LNG  due  to  their  growing  energy  demand  and  desire  to  diversify 

supply sources. Mexico relied on U.S. gas imports to meet domestic demand as well. 

Thereby, U.S. LNG became one of the key tools of U.S. foreign policy.  

In  2014,  American  energy  diplomacy  moved  on  three  strategic  fronts: 

countering  Russian  energy  penetration  in  Europe,  advancing  international  clean 

energy transition partnerships, and mainstreaming energy security into broader foreign 

policy  frameworks.  In  response  to  the Crimean crisis, the U.S. openly put energy on 

 
63 
the  table  as  a  tool  of  coercion  used  by  Moscow.  A  State  Department  fact  sheet, 

"Russian Fiction the Sequel,"101 showed that Russia increased prices of gas to Ukraine 

by 80%, utilizing energy interdependence as a punishment and weapon against Kiev's 

westward  shift.  In  his  March  26,  2014  speech  in  Brussels,  President  Barack  Obama 

framed  Russia's  ‘annexation  of  Crimea’  not  as  the  start  of a new Cold War, but as a 

turning  point  for  the  international  system  —  an  act  of  unilateral  violence  that 

disregarded the post-World War II legal order (The Guardian, 2014). He ruled out any 

resort to military force, emphasizing instead the primacy of diplomacy, sanctions, and 

multilateral pressure. As he portrayed Russia as increasingly isolated, Obama stressed 

that  economic  and  normative  instruments  alone  would  deliver lasting settlement. He 

equated  energy  policy  with  security,  establishing  clearly  that  American  shale  gas 

exports  could  allow  Europe  to  end  its reliance on Russian energy and therefore strip 

Moscow  of  its  coercive  influence  (ibid.).  He  also  urged  Europe  to  undertake  tough 

domestic  transformations,  like  fracking,  in  order  to  develop  native  energy  capacity 

(ibid.).  Obama's  discourse  positioned  the  United  States  as  a  stabilizing  power  with 

liberal  values  and  energy  diversification  as tools of strategic deterrence. The address 

thus  marked  the confluence of energy diplomacy and geopolitical containment in the 

West's  new  rhetoric  vis-à-vis  Russia.  The  April  5th  U.S.-EU  Energy  Council102  was 

the  primary  platform  where  Secretary  John  Kerry  emphasized 

transatlantic 

partnership  to  be  less  reliant  on  Russian  gas.  The  Council  also  supported  new  gas 

interconnections  (e.g.,  Ukraine,  Poland,  Slovakia  routes),  LNG  terminals,  and  the 

Southern  Corridor  project  linking  Azerbaijan  to  Europe.  Kerry  also  emphasized  the 

capacity  of  the  U.S.  to  export  more  gas  than  what  is  being  used  in  Europe  today, 

taking advantage of approved export permits (U.S. Department of State, 2014).

64 

In  addition, on April 28, 2014, the U.S. Department of State imposed103 export 

controls on high-technology defense articles to Russia and occupied Crimea. This was 

a  historic  coordination  between  arms  control  and  energy  sanctions  tools,  explicitly 

limiting Russia's dual-use capability under the U.S. Munitions List. Besides economic 

measures,  the  U.S.  waged  a  war  of  words  to  discredit  Russian  actions.  There  was  a 

series of fact sheets and media notes attributing to the Kremlin instigation of warfare 

via  paramilitary  proxies  and  propaganda.  American  documentation  of  Russian  arms 

shipments,  border  incursions,  and  oil  manipulation  provided  the  foundation  for  an 

aggressive  strategic  communications effort aimed at shaping world public opinion104. 

The  December  2014  Joint  U.S.-EU  Energy  Council  institutionalized  further  this 

agenda with an accord that energy must not be used ‘as a political tool’105. It invoked 

the trilateral Ukraine gas agreement and mentioned U.S. and EU cooperation in order 

to  enable  Ukraine's  integration  into  the  EU  energy  market,  promote  domestic 

production, and expand renewables. 

While  confronting  Russia,  the  U.S.  also  deepened  bilateral  and  multilateral 

clean  energy  partnerships:  with  India106  (expanded the Partnership to Advance Clean 

Energy  (PACE)  initiative,  created  the  “Greening  the  Grid”  project,  and  launched  a 

$1B  Ex-Im  Bank  MOU  to  finance  solar  exports);  with  China107  (joint  research  on 

carbon  capture,  grid  modernization,  and  low-carbon  transport  under  the  U.S.-China 

Climate  Change  Working  Group);  with  Africa108  (increased  U.S.-ACEF  funding  to 

$30  million, enabling micro-grid, solar, and hydroelectric development that unlocked

hundreds  of  millions  in  private  investment);  with  the  Caribbean  and  Eastern

In sum, Russia was consistently depicted as a revisionist actor leveraging its resource 

endowment  for  geopolitical  purposes,  while  the  U.S.  has  become  a  provider  of 

resilience  — offering infrastructure funding, LNG exports, regulatory assistance, and 

diplomatic support to both Europe and emerging markets.  

During 2015, the U.S. government utilized strategic energy diplomacy in an 

attempt to build alliances, assist clean energy transitions, and limit competitors — 

primarily Russia. A key innovation was the launch of the Climate and Clean Energy 

Investment Forum, co-hosted with Google and Georgetown University. The forum 

placed investment as a policy and discursive lever, framing U.S. public-private 

partnerships as scalable alternatives to state-led energy models like Russia's Gazprom. 

Investment instruments included USAID's CEFF-CCA and OPIC's $400 million 

support of South African CSP projects111. This discourse framed energy not simply as 

a commodity, but as a tool of resilience, climate diplomacy, and democratic 

development — implicitly contrasting with Russia’s weaponization of hydrocarbons. 

A number of statements directly condemned Russian interference in Ukraine and 

energy manipulation. The U.S.-Ukraine Energy Security Dialogue112 acknowledged 

technical and financial support for gas diversification, Naftogaz reform, and 

electricity production — a unification of Ukraine's autonomy from Russian fuel 

sources. Simultaneously, the U.S. actively delegitimized Russia's law and influence

efforts, like the ‘Undesirables’ Law113 used to push out NGOs such as the Open 

Society Foundations. These actions extended U.S. influence not only in the energy 

sector but also into the broader arena of civil society and dominance over information 

space. A background briefing on ISIL oil revenues114 directly refuted Russian claims 

that Turkey had been engaged in illicit oil trade. U.S. officials provided technical 

refutations of Russia's satellite imagery, depriving Moscow of its moral authority 

while reasserting U.S. commitment to tracking and disabling ISIL sources of revenue 

through oil infrastructure targeting. Additionally, in upper-level interactions between 

Kerry and Lavrov/Putin115, U.S. rhetoric116 paired Russia's strategic behaviors (e.g. air 

strikes, energy deals, Ukrainian destabilization) with the imperative of rule-based 

solutions — emphasizing UN processes, ceasefire monitoring, and constitutional 

reform over unilateral energy sway. 

In  2016,  U.S.  foreign  energy  policy  deepened  its  two-track  strategy: 

encouraging  energy  security  and  climate  cooperation  with  allies  and  partners  and

marked  a  paradigm  shift:  climate  and  energy  diplomacy  became  the  tools  of  global 

strategic  leadership.  The  twin  backing  of  the  two  countries  of  ETS  development, 

management of transport sector emissions, and reforming the Montreal Protocol HFC 

made  both  of  them  the  co-managers  of  the  low-carbon  transition.  Multilevel 

governance  (national,  subnational,  and  private)  aided  America's  desires  for 

polycentric  leadership.  In  the  U.S.-Caribbean  and  Central  American  Energy 

Summit120  and  U.S.-Guatemala  Energy  Dialogue121,  Washington  promoted  regional 

energy  integration,  particularly  through  transborder  interconnection  and  the  use  of 

natural  gas.  They  were  neoliberal  integration  model-based,  coupling  market 

deregulation  with  restructuring  of  the  regulatory  system.  Kerry  linked  energy 

modernization  with  democratization  and  stability,  thus  locating  it  as  part  of  deeper 

goals  of  democratization.  In  Africa,  the  U.S.-Angola  Strategic  Energy  Dialogue122 

emphasized regulatory improvement and environmental performance. 

Energy  politics  remained at the center of the Syrian conflict. U.S.-Russia talks 

repeatedly  evoked  humanitarian  access  and civilian infrastructure protection, both of 

which  cover  critical  energy  infrastructure  (water, electricity, fuel convoys). The U.S. 

positioned  itself  as  a humanitarian norm-defender and Russia as blocking, leading to 

the  October  closure  of  bilateral  Syria channels123. The U.S.-Cuba Renewable Energy

3.3  2017-2021:  Trump  Administration,  Energy  Independence  and  Energy 

Dominance 

The  2017-2021  was  an  assertive  period,  where  the  instruments  of  hard power 

and  soft  power  were  employed.  In  2017,  President  Donald  Trump  redefined  the 

direction  of  U.S.  energy  policy  by  introducing  the concept of ‘energy dominance,’ a 

term  aimed  at  distinguishing  his  administration’s  agenda  from  Obama’s.  Trump 

announced a ‘golden era’ of American energy: it was about to be driven by increased 

fossil  fuel  production  and  the  increased  export  of  coal,  oil,  and  LNG,  especially  to 

markets  in  Eastern  Europe  and  Asia  (DiChristopher,  2017).  Energy  Secretary  Rick 

Perry defined energy dominance as: “An energy dominant America means self-reliant. 

It means a secure nation, free from the geopolitical turmoil of other nations who seek 

to  use  energy  as  an  economic  weapon.”  and  “An  energy  dominant  America  will 

export  to  markets  around  the  world,  increasing  our  global  leadership  and  our 

influence.”127  These  citations  were  published  by  CNBC  on  July  1  of  2017.  Critics, 

however, questioned the coherence of this strategy, pointing to Trump’s simultaneous 

cuts  to  clean  energy  R&D  and  his  withdrawal  from  the  Paris  Agreement,  which 

undercut  the  global  credibility  of  U.S.  leadership  in  sustainable  energy  (ibid.). 

Overall, during President Donald Trump the United States used sanctions, diplomatic 

pressure, encouragement of LNG exports, and strategic framing of Russian energy as 

a threat to trigger Europe's exit from reliance on Russia. 

In  practical  terms,  2017  saw  the  U.S.  begin  ramping  up  exports  of  LNG  to 

overseas markets. U.S. LNG exports quadrupled from 2016 levels to reach about 1.94 

billion  cubic  feet  per  day in 2017128, with cargoes reaching 25 countries across Asia, 

Europe,  the  Middle  East,  and  the  Americas.  Notably,  Europe  emerged  as  a  growing 

127  DiChristopher,  T.  (2017,  July  1).  Trump  wants America to be ‘energy dominant.’ Here’s what that means. CNBC. 
https://www.cnbc.com/2017/06/28/trump-america-energy-dominant-policy.html  
128  U.S.  Energy  Information Administration. (2018, March 27). U.S. liquefied natural gas exports quadrupled in 2017. 
https://www.eia.gov/todayinenergy/detail.php?id=35512#:~:text=U.S.%20exports%20of%20liquefied%20natural,0.5%2
0Bcf%2Fd%20in%202016  

 
70 
destination (the third-largest regional importer of U.S. LNG in 2017), reflecting initial 

transatlantic objectives of diversifying gas supplies, according to EIA (2018). Even as 

the  United  States  promoted  its  LNG  as  a  source  of  ‘freedom’  and  security,  it 

simultaneously  employed  hard  power  measures  against  Russian  energy  ventures.  In 

August 2017, Congress passed and President Trump signed the Countering America’s 

Adversaries  Through  Sanctions  Act  (CAATSA).  This  sweeping  sanctions  law  —  a 

response to Russian ‘interference’ in Ukraine and the 2016 U.S. election — explicitly 

targeted  Russia’s  energy  sector  and  threatened  secondary  sanctions  on  foreign 

companies  assisting  Russian  export  pipelines.  CAATSA’s  Section  257  on  Ukrainian 

energy  security  identified  projects  like  Nord  Stream  2  as  a  particular  concern  that 

threatens energy security of Europe. It is states that it is the policy of the USA ‘to help 

Ukraine  and  United  States  allies  and  partners  in  Europe  reduce  their dependence on 

Russian  energy  resources,  especially  natural  gas,  which  the  Government  of  the 

Russian  Federation  uses  as  a  weapon  to  coerce,  intimidate,  and  influence  other 

countries’129  — very clear anti-Russian discourse   positioning it as an aggressor state 

that  weaponizes  its  energy  industry  against  European  countries.  The  law  thus 

represented  an  early  hard-power  instrument  to  disable  Moscow’s  energy  ambitions 

and  support  Europe’s  energy  diversification  (even  as  its  implementation  would  be 

calibrated  in  deference  to  European  sensitivities).  Diplomatically,  2017  also  saw 

high-profile  U.S.  efforts  to  encourage  European  nations  to  buy  American  energy. In 

July,  President  Trump  visited  Poland  and  the  Three  Seas  Initiative  summit130, where 

he praised Poland for receiving its first U.S. LNG shipment that month and promised 

that the United States could provide Central Europe with alternative fuel. In Warsaw, 

Trump  vowed  ‘America  stands  ready  to  help  Poland  and  other  European  nations 

129 U.S. Congress. (2017). Countering America's Adversaries Through Sanctions Act, Pub. L. No. 115–44, 131 Stat. 886. 
https://www.congress.gov/115/plaws/publ44/PLAW-115publ44.pdf  
130  The  White  House.  (2017,  July  6).  Remarks  by  President  Trump  at  the  Three  Seas  Initiative  Summit. 
https://2017-2021-translations.state.gov/2017/07/06/remarks-by-president-trump-at-the-three-seas-initiative-summit-july
-6-2017/  

 
71 
diversify  their  energy  supplies’131,  so  that  they  are  ‘never  again  held  hostage  to  a 

single  supplier  or nation’132 — a clear reference to Russia. The Polish leadership, for 

its  part,  welcomed  the  U.S.  LNG  as  a  vehicle  for  supply  diversification,  with 

President Andrzej Duda hailing the inaugural cargo and expressing hope for long-term 

contracts to import U.S. gas as a way to reduce Poland’s reliance on Russian imports. 

Through  such  soft-power  signaling  and  LNG  diplomacy,  the  U.S.  began  to  frame 

itself  as  the  reliable  partner  enabling  Europe’s  ‘gas  freedom,’  in  contrast  to  the 

coercive supplier in Moscow. 

In 2018, the U.S. escalated its campaign both rhetorically and diplomatically to 

persuade  European  allies  to  rethink  their  dependence  on  Russian  energy.  A  pivotal 

moment  came  at  the  NATO  summit  in  July  2018,  when  President  Trump  publicly 

warned  Germany  for  the  Nord  Stream  2  pipeline.  In  an  unusually  blunt  critique 

delivered  at  a  televised  breakfast  with  NATO’s  Secretary  General,  Trump  claimed 

“Germany is totally controlled by Russia”133 because of its heavy reliance on Russian 

gas, noting “they will be getting 60 to 70 percent of their energy from Russia” via the 

new  pipeline  (Trump,  2018)  as  cited  in  PBS  News.  He  questioned  how  Germany 

could  be  secure  while  its  energy  lifeline  was  in  Putin’s  hands,  calling  the  situation 

“inappropriate”  and  “a  very  bad  thing  for  NATO”  (ibid.).  This  very  public claim — 

albeit with exaggerated figures — underscored the U.S. narrative that Russian energy 

projects  like  Nord  Stream  2  were  undermining  European  security  from  within. 

Washington was effectively securitizing energy in the transatlantic discourse, framing 

gas  pipelines  as  strategic  threats.  Concurrently,  the  Trump  administration  pursued  a 

more  cooperative  avenue  with  the  EU  to  promote  U.S.  LNG.  On  July  25,  2018, 

President Trump and European Commission President Jean-Claude Juncker reached a 

131  The  White  House.  (2017,  July  6).  The  President's  news  conference  with  President  Andrzej  Duda  of  Poland  in

132 DiChristopher, T. (2017, July 6). Trump pitches US natural gas to European leaders, suggests Russian gas holds them

https://www.pbs.org/newshour/politics/at-nato-trump-says-germany-is-totally-controlled-by-russia  

 
72 
surprise  joint  announcement  to  defuse  a  possible  trade  war.  As  part  of  the  resulting 

U.S.-EU deal, the European Union agreed to “strengthen its strategic cooperation with 

the  U.S.  on  energy”134  and  expressed  intent  to  import  more  American  LNG  to 

diversify  its  supplies  (European  Commission,  2018).  Trump  claimed  that  the  EU 

would become a “massive buyer of LNG”135 from the United States and that America 

would  make  it  easier  for  Europe  to  access  this  fuel  (The  White  House,  2018).  The 

joint  statement explicitly noted the EU’s desire to “import more liquefied natural gas 

from  the  United  States  to  diversify  [its]  energy  supply”,  aligning  with  a  core  U.S. 

objective  (ibid.).  Following  this  agreement,  U.S.  officials  and  embassies  in  Europe 

actively  promoted  LNG  trade.  EU  imports  of  U.S.  LNG  did  increase  markedly  - by 

over  180%  year-on-year  —  in  the  wake  of  these  political  commitments  (EU 

Commission,  2019).  The  energy  cooperation  pledge  of  2018  thus  represented  U.S. 

economic statecraft at work: America leveraged its trade negotiations to open markets 

for its gas, reinforcing Europe’s turn toward supply diversification. 

U.S.  diplomacy  in  2018  also  targeted  specific  regions  and  projects  to  counter 

Russian  influence.  The  State  Department’s  energy  envoys  and  the  Department  of 

Energy  pressed  Eastern Mediterranean and Balkan countries to pursue alternatives to 

Russian  gas.  For  instance,  in  Greece  and  Croatia,  the  U.S.  advocated  for  LNG 

terminals  and  new  interconnectors  (such  as  the  Krk  island  LNG  terminal  and  the 

Greece-Bulgaria interconnector) with the promise that the U.S. LNG could be part of 

the supply mix. In September 2018, at the Three Seas Initiative summit in Bucharest, 

U.S.  Energy  Secretary  Rick  Perry  declared  support  for  Central  European 

infrastructure  that  would  allow  American  gas  to  flow  north-south.  During  visits  to 

countries  like  Hungary, Ukraine, and Poland, Secretary Perry consistently hammered
