Joint 

 
73 
home  Washington’s  message  about  the  Kremlin’s  energy  coercion.  “We  should  no 

longer  allow  the  Kremlin  to  use  energy  as  a  weapon,”136  Perry  warned  on  a  trip  to 

Budapest in November 2018, stressing the need for Central Europe to diversify away 

from  Russian  gas  (Reuters,  2018).  This  forceful  informational  framing  by  a  senior 

U.S.  official  reinforced  the  securitization  narrative:  Russia’s  pipeline  projects  were 

cast  as  geopolitical  aggressions, while U.S. energy exports were framed as liberating 

allies.  

By  2019,  American LNG exports surged to record highs in 2019, and the U.S. 

government  promoted  these  exports  as  a  force  for  good.  In  a famous Department of 

Energy  press  release  in  May  2019,  officials  rebranded  natural  gas  in  ideological 

terms:  the  DOE  called  increased  LNG  export  capacity  as  critical  to  “spreading 

freedom gas throughout the world by giving America’s allies a diverse and affordable 

source  of  clean  energy”137  (DOE,  2019).  The  press  release  even  referred  to  gas 

molecules  as  “molecules  of  U.S.  freedom”  being  sent  abroad.  This  deliberate 

messaging  strategy  framed  U.S.  energy  as  not  just  a  commodity  but  a  symbol  of 

liberal  values  and liberation from authoritarian influence — soft power in the energy 

domain.  Beyond  Europe,  the  U.S.  also  pushed  this  narrative  in  Asia,  promoting 

American LNG to Japan, South Korea, and others as a reliable alternative to regional 

competitors. By positioning itself as the arsenal of energy democracy, the U.S. aimed 

to win hearts and minds and secure long-term markets for its exports. 

On the hard-power front, 2019 was a watershed year because the United States 

took  direct  aim  at  the  completion  of  Nord  Stream  2.  After  diplomatic  warning  and 

threats  failed  to  slow  the  project,  the  U.S.  Congress  passed  the  Protecting  Europe’s 

136  Reuters.  (2018,  November  13).  Russia  should  not  be  allowed  to  use  energy  as  a  weapon  –  Perry. 
https://www.reuters.com/article/hungary-us-energy/russia-should-not-be-allowed-to-use-energy-as-a-weapon-perry-idIN
L8N1XO6HH/  
137 U.S. Department of Energy. (2019, May 29). Department of Energy authorizes additional LNG exports from Freeport 
LNG. https://www.energy.gov/articles/department-energy-authorizes-additional-lng-exports-freeport-lng  

 
74 
Energy  Security  Act  (PEESA)138  —  as  a part of National Defense Authorization Act 

for  FY2020.  It  mandated  sanctions  on  any  vessel  engaged  in  pipe-laying  for  Nord 

Stream  2  (or  TurkStream),  effectively  targeting  the  specialty  contractors  needed  to 

finish  the  Baltic  Sea  pipeline.  President  Trump  signed  these  sanctions  into  law  in 

December  2019.  The  impact  was  immediate:  within  days,  the  Swiss  contractor 

Allseas,  which  was  laying  the  pipes,  suspended  its  operations  to  avoid  U.S. 

punishment.  Construction  of  Nord  Stream  2  ground  to  a  halt  with  roughly  160 

kilometers left unfinished. The U.S. had successfully imposed a de facto veto on a key 

Russian-European  energy  link,  demonstrating  Washington’s  willingness  to  use 

legal-regulatory  tools  in  support of its strategic aims. Strategically, the 2019 pipeline 

sanctions  sent  a powerful signal well beyond Nord Stream 2. They conveyed to both 

European governments and private companies that the United States would intervene 

— even at the cost of friction with allies like Germany — to block energy projects it 

deemed  threats  to  allied  security.  It  reinforced the credibility of U.S. commitment to 

Central  and  Eastern  Europe’s  energy  security,  bolstering  those  countries’  bargaining 

position  inside  the  EU. From Moscow’s perspective, these sanctions underscored the 

need  to  seek  alternative markets and routes (which Russia accelerated by pivoting to 

China and Turkey for gas exports). Indeed, December 2019 also saw the inauguration 

of the Power of Siberia pipeline that delivers Russian gas to China — called by Putin 

as  the  start  of  Russia’s  ‘Pivot  to  Asia’  —  partly  reflecting  Russia’s  response  to 

challenges  in  the  west.  Thus,  even  as  the  U.S.  assisted  Europe’s  pivot  away  from 

Russia,  Russia  began  diversifying  toward  Asian  markets,  a  development  outside  the 

U.S.-EU theater but indicative of global consequences. 

In  addition,  in  2019,  the  United  States  utilized  its  energy  influence  elsewhere 

globally,  piling  up  influence  with  sanctions  on  competitive  producers.  Specifically, 

138  U.S.  Congress.  (2019).  H.R.3206  –  Protecting  Europe’s  Energy  Security  Act  of  2019,  116th  Cong. 
https://www.congress.gov/bill/116th-congress/house-bill/3206/text  

 
75 
sweeping sanctions on Venezuela's state oil company (PdVSA)139, constraining one of 

Russia's  allied  shippers  of  oil and redirecting some supply chains. These hard-power 

moves  tightened  global  supply  and  indirectly increased demand for U.S. oil and that 

of its partners. By the end of 2019, American oil output reached an all-time high (over 

12 million barrels per day) (EIA, 2019) and U.S. crude exports hit record levels, some 

of  which  went  to  Europe  to  replace  sanctioned  barrels.  In  fact,  as  mentioned  in  the 

beginning of the chapter, 2019 marked the first time in 67 years that the United States 

exported more total energy than it imported, symbolically achieving a form of ‘energy 

independence’  that  successive  presidents  had  sought.  This  newfound  position  of 

strength enabled the U.S. to act as a swing player in global energy geopolitics — from 

Europe  to  Asia  and Latin America — by flexing sanctions and offering supply. Still, 

the  U.S.  approach  was  not  purely  self-serving;  American  officials  framed  these 

actions  as  aligning  economic  interests  with  a  principled  stand  for  a  free  and  secure 

energy order.  

The  year  2020  brought  unprecedented  turbulence  to  energy  markets  with  the 

COVID-19  pandemic,  yet  the  United  States  maintained  its  strategic  push  on  energy 

security  issues.  The  pandemic-triggered  collapse  in  oil  and  gas  demand  led  to 

temporary  oversupply  and  historically  low  prices,  testing  the  viability  of  the  U.S. 

shale  industry.  The  Trump  administration  stayed  focused  on  countering  Russian 

energy  influence.  In  fact,  as  Russia  and  Germany  attempted  to  circumvent  U.S. 

sanctions  to  complete  Nord  Stream  2,  Washington  doubled  down  on  its  hard-power 

measures.  In  July  2020,  U.S.  Secretary  of  State  Mike  Pompeo  announced  the 

termination  of  a  sanctions  waiver  that  had  previously  spared  Nord  Stream  2  from 

certain  CAATSA  penalties.  Pompeo  updated  the  public  guidance  on  CAATSA  to 

explicitly  include  Nord  Stream  2  and  the  second  line  of  TurkStream,  warning 

139  U.S.  Department  of  the  Treasury.  (2019,  January  28).  Treasury  sanctions  Venezuela’s  state-owned  oil  company 
Petroleos de Venezuela, S.A. https://home.treasury.gov/news/press-releases/sm594  

 
76 
companies  aiding  those  projects  to  “get  out  now  or  risk  the  consequences”140.  The 

strategic  effect  was  to  delay  the  pipeline  further  and  to  raise  the  political  costs  for 

Germany  and  Russia.  Alongside  sanctions,  the  U.S.  in  2020  increased  diplomatic 

coordination  with  European  partners  to  manage  the  fallout and maintain pressure on 

Russia.  The  State  Department  and  Congress  engaged  in  intensive  dialogue  with 

Germany,  trying  to  convince  Berlin  to  pause  or  cancel  Nord  Stream  2.  Although 

Germany  publicly  protested  against  U.S.  extraterritorial  sanctions  as  interference,  it 

also  began  hedging;  i.e.,  German  authorities  discussed  national  and  EU  regulatory 

alternatives  that  would  limit  Gazprom's  control  over  the pipeline if constructed. The 

United  States  could  count  on  steadfast  support  from  Poland  and  the  Baltic  states, 

which  together  comprised  a  de facto coalition with Washington against Nord Stream 

2.  Apart  from  that,  U.S.  diplomacy  highlighted  alternatives  to  Europe:  officials 

pushed  the  Southern  Gas  Corridor,  which  in  2020  was  nearing  completion  to  bring 

Azerbaijani  gas  to  Italy,  and  they  peddled  new  U.S.  LNG  trades (indeed, U.S. LNG 

exports  to  Europe  did  continue,  and  volumes  later  in  the  year  rebounded  as  prices 

did). Even through the pandemic, U.S. LNG exports held up — flexible U.S. cargoes 

assisted  in  refilling  European  gas  storage  by  late  2020,  demonstrating  the  merit  of 

having  the  U.S.  as  a  supplier  in  times  of  volatility.  Such  market-driven  confidence 

reinforced the diplomatic message that Europe would be more secure with diversified 

supplies. 

U.S.  policy  in  2020  also  paid  attention  to Asia and other regions: Asia EDGE 

(Enhancing  Development  and  Growth  through  Energy)141,  prepared  in  mid-2018, 

gained momentum by 2020. Through Asia EDGE, the U.S. worked with countries like 

India,  Japan,  and  Vietnam  to  promote  sustainable  and  secure  energy  markets  — 

implicitly encouraging them to choose U.S. LNG, renewables, and nuclear technology 

140 U.S. Department of State. (2020, July 15). Secretary Michael R. Pompeo at a press availability (Remarks by Michael 
R.  Pompeo,  Secretary  of  State,  and  Francis  R.  Fannon,  Assistant  Secretary,  Bureau  of  Energy  Resources). 
https://2017-2021.state.gov/secretary-michael-r-pompeo-at-a-press-availability-9/  
141  U.S.  Department  of  State.  (n.d.).  Asia  EDGE  –  Enhancing  Development  and  Growth  through  Energy. 
https://2017-2021.state.gov/asia-edge/  

 
77 
over Chinese or Russian options. In February 2020, the U.S. and India upgraded their 

Strategic  Energy  Partnership142,  with  India  agreeing  to  greater  U.S.  oil  and  LNG 

imports, thereby reducing its reliance on Middle Eastern and Russian sources. In Latin 

America,  the  U.S.  continued  attempts  to  isolate  Venezuela  and  its  oil  regime  and 

instead  deepened  cooperation  in  energy  with  Brazil,  Colombia,  and  others  aligned 

with  U.S.  interests.  The  U.S.  hard  power  tools  were  still  evolving  to  meet  the 

perceived  challenge  from  Russian  energy  projects.  In  parallel,  however,  there  was 

recognition  in  Washington  that  European  allies’  own  decisions  would  ultimately 

dictate outcomes. As one State Department official acknowledged, Nord Stream 2 was 

over 90% built by the time the U.S. fully engaged, making “stopping it a long shot”143. 

This  pragmatic  note  hinted  at  the  limits  of  U.S.  influence  and  set  the  stage  for  a 

rethinking under the upcoming Biden administration. 

In 2021, U.S. policy on global energy markets saw a transition with the change 

in  administration,  but  many underlying objectives remained consistent. President Joe 

Biden  took  office  in  January  2021  with  a  priority  to  “rehabilitating  frayed 

alliances”144,  especially  with  core  EU  partners  like  Germany,  even  as  he  shared  his 

predecessor’s  skepticism  of  Russian  energy  projects.  The  Biden  administration 

immediately continued to oppose Nord Stream 2 but with a task to not undermine the 

U.S.-German relationship. This led to a use of both hard and soft power. In May 2021, 

the State Department issued a report identifying the company building Nord Stream 2 

(Nord  Stream  2  AG)  and  its  CEO  as  engaged  in  sanctionable  activity,  yet Secretary 

Blinken  simultaneously  waived  those  particular  sanctions,  citing  U.S.  national

interests  (to  maintain  unity  with  Germany) (Shalal, Gardner, & Holland, 2021). This 

controversial decision essentially conceded that the pipeline would be completed, but 

sought  to preserve leverage and goodwill for the post-completion phase. At the same 

time,  the  U.S.  did  impose  sanctions  on  a  number  of  Russian  vessels  and  firms 

involved  in  the  construction,  to  signal  continued  disapproval.  The  mixed  approach 

reflected an attempt at coercive diplomacy: using enough sanctions to emphasize U.S. 

opposition,  while  using  waivers  as an olive branch to Germany. Over the summer of 

2021,  intensive  U.S.-German  negotiations  culminated  in  a  Joint  Statement  on  Nord 

Stream  2145.  In  this  agreement,  Germany  pledged  to  take  national-level  action 

(including  potential  sanctions)  if  Russia  used  energy  as  a  weapon  or  committed 

further aggression against Ukraine, and it committed funding to help Ukraine’s energy 

transition  and  security.  The  United  States,  in  turn,  accepted  the  completion  of  Nord 

Stream  2  but  stressed  that  the  pipeline  should  not  be  allowed  to  endanger  Europe’s 

security.  Essentially,  the  U.S.  leveraged  soft  power  and  alliance  relations  to  extract 

German  commitments  that  aligned  with  the  original  goal  of  protecting  Ukraine  and 

limiting  Putin’s  leverage.  While  some  Eastern  European  nations  were  unhappy  with 

this  compromise,  it  demonstrated  Washington’s  recognition  that  European  interests 

mattered  —  the  EU’s  largest  economy  had  to  be  on  board  to  truly  counter  Russian 

energy 

influence.  The 

joint  statement  also  showed  a  U.S.  shift 

toward 

multilateralizing the response (making it a Western consensus to deter Russia’s energy 

coercion, rather than a unilateral U.S. campaign). 

Beyond  the  Nord  Stream  saga,  the  U.S.  in  2021  continued  to  promote energy 

diversification  and  climate-security initiatives. The Biden administration rejoined the 

Paris  Climate  Agreement  and  U.S.  officials  continued  supporting  the  Three  Seas 

Initiative and other European efforts to build gas interconnections and LNG terminals. 

During  2021’s  global  gas  supply  crunch  (when  post-COVID  demand  spiked  and

European  spot  gas  prices  hit  record  highs),  the  U.S.  urged  Russia  to  increase  gas 

supplies  to  Europe  and  “do  more  to  ensure  European  energy  security”146.  American 

and  EU  politicians  argued  that  Gazprom’s  failure  to  ship  adequate  gas  exacerbated 

Europe’s  energy  crisis147  —  reinforcing  the  U.S.  narrative that Russia was willing to 

“weaponize”  energy  by exploiting European problems for political gain. At that time 

40% of gas was imported from Russia in the EU and 60% of imported gas was used to 

power houses and factories (The Moscow Times, 2021). These events set the stage for 

a transatlantic alignment: by late 2021, EU member states were far more vocal about 

the risks of over-reliance on Russian gas.  

On  the  global  stage,  the  U.S.  also  underscored  its  role  as  a  supplier  of  last 

resort.  By  December  2021,  the  United  States  had  become  the  world’s  largest  LNG 

exporter  on  a  monthly  basis,  with  massive  volumes  flowing  to  Asia  (particularly 

China, South Korea, and Japan) where gas demand was surging. This made the U.S. a 

critical  player  in  balancing  Asia-Europe  gas  dynamics:  for  instance,  when  Asian 

buyers  bid  away  LNG  cargoes,  Europe  faced  shortages  —  a  predicament  that 

highlighted the interdependence of Atlantic and Pacific energy security. The U.S. also 

convened  the  Quad  energy  working  group148  (with  India,  Japan, Australia) to ensure 

that Indo-Pacific allies support decarbonization efforts.  

In  sum,  the  2017-2021  period  saw the United States reassert itself as a central 

actor  in  global  energy  geopolitics,  employing  a  wide  array  of  instruments  to  steer 

allies away from Russian energy dependence and toward a diversified, U.S.-inclusive 

supply  structure.  Washington’s  use  of  hard  power  (e.g.  sanctions  on  pipelines),  soft 

power  (high-level  diplomacy  and  normative  framing),  economic  leverage  (LNG

outcomes  in  Europe’s  energy  pivot.  By  2021,  the  EU  was  more  actively  pursuing 

energy  security  measures  long  advocated  by  the  U.S.,  such  as  building  LNG 

terminals,  enacting  stricter  regulations  on  pipeline ownership, and integrating its gas 

networks.  At  the  same time, the limits of U.S. influence were evident: some projects 

(like  Nord  Stream  2)  moved  forward  due  to  strong  vested  interests  in  Europe, 

reminding  that Europe’s own calculations (economic costs, political trade-offs) could 

override  U.S.  preferences.  In  essence,  U.S.  influence  was  significant  but  not  solely 

deterministic.  It  worked  best  in  synergy  with  European  stakeholders  who  shared 

American  threat  perceptions,  and  it  encountered  pushback  when  U.S.  tactics  were 

seen as too unilateral.  

 
 
3.4 2022-2024: LNG Leadership 

81 

The  year  2022 began with continued concerns of Western hemisphere over the 

Ukraine  situation  and  Russian  mobilisation  of  resources  near  borders,  which  was 

named  a  “deeply  troubling  build-up”149.  Secretary  Blinken  in  January  2022  claimed 

that  Nord  Stream  operation  is  strongly  opposed  by  the  USA  as  it  “undermined 

Europe’s energy security”150, especially damaging Ukraine by depriving it of potential 

transit  fees  economic  benefit,  as  pipeline  bypasses  Ukraine’s  territory.  In  general  in 

many  statements151  by  U.S.  officials  there  is  a  discourse  of  calling  Russia  to 

diplomatic  resolution  of  the  Ukraine  conflict  (before  actual  aggression),  referring  to 

difficulties of Ukraine and European energy security as a whole152. Many arguments153 

were  based  on  positioning  Russia  as  a  liar  and  not  a  credible  partner  that  does  not 

deserve  any  trust,  but  only  spreads misinformation about its history and problems of 

people  of  Donbas154.  Russia  is  framed  as  a  dangerous  adversary  that  destabilizes 

neighbours  internal affairs and undermines sovereignty155, these claims are supported 

by  recalling  Russian  ‘interference’  in  U.S.  2016  elections,  of  which  Russia  is  still 

accused  by  the  USA, despite absence of factual proof. Blinken continuously claimed 

NATO to be a defensive alliance that ‘didn’t invade Georgia… didn’t invade Ukraine.

The February 2022 Joint Statement of the U.S.-EU Energy Council emphasizes 

‘resilience,’  ‘partnership,’  and  a  ‘just  energy  transition’157.  The  U.S.  framed itself as 

Europe’s guarantor of energy security in the face of Russian coercion, advocating for 

supply diversification, and infrastructure synchronization with Ukraine and Moldova. 

Simultaneously,  it  promoted  liberal  energy  governance  norms  —  such  as  regulatory 

transparency and market integration — particularly in Eastern Europe and the Western 

Balkans,  thereby  linking  decarbonization  to  democratic  reform.  Antony  J.  Blinken 

claimed in a talk with Vice President of the European Commission Josep Borrell, and 

European  Commissioner  for  Energy  Kadri  Simson  at  the  U.S.-EU  Energy  Council

people  through  the  winter,  Russia  has  attempted  to  increase  its  energy  leverage”158, 

and Borrell supported it: ‘Russia don’t hesitate on using the energy supplies to Europe 

as  a  weapon  for  geopolitical  gain’.  These  statements  frame  Russia  as  an  aggressor 

using  energy as a coercive geopolitical tool. In their discourse, recallings of previous 

Russian actions, such as the 2009 crisis, are present: ‘When Russia halted gas supplies

On  February  24,  the  anticipated  Ukraine  crisis  happened.  The  CSIS  report 

“U.S.  LNG:  Remapping  Energy  Security”  examines  the  significant  shift  in  global 

energy  dynamics  following  the  initiation  of  special  military operation, showing how 

Europe  expanded  renewable  energy  initiatives,  and  increased  imports  of  LNG  from 

the  United  States. Despite economic strain on European industries, more costly USA 

LNG  was  still  considered  a  better  alternative  to  Russian  supplies.  U.S.  natural  gas 

exports  have  become  crucial  for  European  energy security (CSIS, 2023). The rise of 

U.S.  LNG  posed  a  direct  challenge  to  Russia’s  dominance  in  the  European  gas 

market. Russian response included expanding its own LNG capacity and seeking new 

markets in Asia. However, U.S. LNG’s flexible pricing and delivery model made it an 

attractive option for many buyers, leading to an increased competition in key markets. 

U.S.  LNG  exports  helped  meet  rising  demand  in  countries  like  China  and  India, 

reducing  their  reliance  on  Middle  Eastern  and  Russian  gas,  so  this  fast-growing 

market became a new field of U.S.-Russia competition.  

As  a  reaction  to  the  Ukraine  crisis,  the  USA  answered  with  immediate 

sanctions160,  so did the EU. The USA also paused participation in all meetings of the 

Arctic  Council  and  its  subsidiary  bodies161  and  suspended  Russia’s  Permanent

Recognition  of  Donetsk  and  Luhansk  People’s  Republics  was  called  by  Blinken  a 

‘blatant  violation  of  international  law’165  —  supporting  anti-Russia  discourse  as  an 

unreliable  actor. G7 Foreign Ministers’ Statement on Russia and Ukraine highlighted 

that ‘Russia’s unprovoked and unjustified massing of military forces is a challenge to 

global  security  and  the  international  order.’166  —  showing  Russia  as  a  destabilizing 

actor. In response to Russia’s special military operation in Ukraine, the United States 

adopted a multidimensional sanctions package aimed at degrading the Kremlin’s ‘war 

capacity’  and  punishing  its  political  and  economic  elite.  Sanctions  were  aimed  at

engagement on Ukraine as both a reaffirmation of American leadership and a defense 

of  the  rules-based  international  order169.  Through discourse that emphasizes Russia’s 

isolation,  illegitimacy,  and  immorality,  the U.S. promotes institutions like the United 

Nations  as  arenas  for  collective  resistance  against  aggression.  By  mobilizing  allies 

across  multiple  UN  bodies  and  framing  this crisis as an assault on global norms, the 

U.S.  positions  itself  as  a  moral  leader  coordinating  institutional  responses  to 

international  security  threats.  In  a  March  2022  statement,  Secretary  of  State Antony 

Blinken  formally  declared  that  “members  of  Russia’s  forces  have  committed  war 

crimes  in  Ukraine,”  marking  a  significant  escalation  in  U.S.  discourse  condemning 

Russia’s  military  conduct.  The  United  States,  basing  its  assessment  on  public  and 

intelligence  sources,  committed  to  pursuing  accountability  “using  every  tool 

available,”170  signaling  intent  to  coordinate  with  international  legal  mechanisms  and 

partners  to  hold  perpetrators  responsible.  Accusations  of  illegal  cybersecurity 

activities  were  also  present171.  Led  by  Under  Secretary  Victoria  Nuland  and  EEAS 

Deputy  Secretary  General  Enrique  Mora,  the  first  U.S.-EU  High-Level  Dialogue  on 

Russia,  held in Washington, reinforced “exceptional unity” between the EU and U.S.

Paris  Agreement  that  sets  aim  to  achieve  a  net-zero  economy  by  2050,  and 

participation  in  the  Earth  Day  2022183  as  well  as  consensus  at  the  UN  Environment 

Assembly  to  create  a  negotiating  process  to  end  plastic  pollution  —  these  actions 

support  the  general  commitment  of  the  U.S.  to  tackle  climate  change  and  support  a 

better  environment  for  a  sustainable  future.  However,  in  2022  the  USA  was  still  a 

second  state  after  China  contributing  to  CO2  emissions  and  thus  greenhouse  effect, 

that it called to deal with (Paddison & Choi, 2024). 

In 2023-2024 the United States aggressively leveraged its status as a major gas 

exporter  to  influence  world  markets.  In  2023  80%  of  Germany  LNG  imports  came 

from  the  U.S.184  European  countries  ‘benefit’  from  the  decreasing  dependence  on 

Russian  imports  in  context  of  crisis  and  instability,  while  Asian  allies  benefit  from 

LNG  as  they  try  to  decrease  coal  consumption,  highlighting  the  success  and  global 

reach of U.S. gas diplomacy — in fact, it is the U.S. that benefits the most. The Biden 

administration  publicly  committed  to  helping  Europe  diversify  away  from  Russian 

gas,  including  targets  set with EU partners (e.g. aiming for ~50 bcm/yr of U.S. LNG 

to  Europe  by  2030)185.  However,  the  U.S.  also  balanced  this  with  environmental 

concerns. In January 2024 the Department of Energy “paused” approval of new LNG 

export  projects  to  conduct  a  climate  review.  President  Biden  stressed that this pause 

would allow study of emissions impacts and “sees the climate crisis for what it is: the 

existential threat of our time”186.

89 

A central pillar of U.S. strategy was to tighten sanctions on Russia’s oil and gas 

sector. In late 2024, the State Department announced “fresh sanctions” on entities tied 

to  the  Nord  Stream 2  pipeline,  re-designating  numerous  companies  involved  in 

construction  and  operation187.  Similarly,  in  November  2024  the  U.S.  imposed 

sanctions  on  dozens  of  firms  and  vessels  connected  to  Russia’s  Arctic  LNG 2 

project188.  By  targeting  the  contractors,  carriers  and  insurers  supporting  that  plant, 

Washington  sought  to  block  Russian  plans  to  expand  LNG  exports  in  the  future. 

These actions complemented existing measures: since early 2022 the U.S. had applied 

price‑cap  regimes  on  Russian  oil  and  banned  services  for  Soviet‑era  pipelines.  The 

2023-24  round  of  sanctions  thus  deepened  the  squeeze  on  Moscow’s  hydrocarbon 

revenues. In Congressional and executive statements, U.S. policymakers underscored 

that sanctions would “ratchet up the risk” of doing business in Russia’s oil industry189, 

ensuring  that  Western  capital  and  technology  could  not  easily  reinvigorate  Russian 

fields.  

The  Biden  administration  consistently  portrayed  itself  as  combining  energy 

security  with  environmental  responsibility.  For  example,  at  COP28  in  Dubai  U.S. 

officials  unveiled  stringent  methane  rules  for  the  oil  and  gas  sector.  Vice  President 

Harris  cited  these  measures  to  declare  that the United States had restored the United 

States  as  a  global  leader in the fight against climate change. Similarly, in April 2023 

President  Biden  convened  the  Major  Economies  Forum  on  Energy  and  Climate  and 

announced major finance commitments for developing countries. He pledged an extra 

$1  billion  to  the  Green  Climate  Fund  (doubling  the  U.S.  share)  and  proposed  $0.5

Beyond  Europe,  the  U.S.  extended  its  energy  influence  through  targeted 

outreach  in  Asia,  Africa  and  the  Americas.  In  Asia,  the  administration  deepened 

collaboration  with  key  buyers  and  partners.  At  the  Summit  for  a  New  Global 

Financing Pact in March 2023, the U.S. secured backing from Japan and South Korea 

to  increase  renewable  energy  finance  in  Asia.  Official  visits  underscored  this:  for 

example,  in  June  2023 President Biden and Prime Minister Modi announced a broad 

technology  and  climate  partnership.  They  launched  joint  initiatives  on  green 

hydrogen,  offshore  wind,  and  critical  minerals  under  a  newly  established  U.S.-India 

“Strategic  Clean  Energy  Partnership”192.  The  U.S.  also  worked  through  regional 

forums:  the  Indo‑Pacific  Strategy  released  in  January  2025  (reflecting  2024 actions) 

explicitly  made  climate  and  clean  energy  a  cornerstone  of  alliances  with  Japan  and 

Australia193.  In  practice  this  meant  U.S.  agencies  and  development  finance  groups 

supported  renewable  projects  and  grid modernization across Asia. For instance, U.S. 

trade  and  development  agencies  provided  loans  and  grants  for  solar  and  LNG

2023  the  White  House  reported  17  senior  delegations  to  26  African  countries, often 

focusing  on  energy  and  climate  issues194.  Overall,  though  Europe  remained  the  top 

destination  for  U.S.  gas,  the  Asia‑Africa‑Latin  engagements  ensured  U.S.  influence 

extended worldwide through project financing, technology partnerships and trade. 

In  2023-24  a  common  theme  was  the  contrast  between  ‘democratic’  versus 

‘autocratic’ energy. The U.S. repeatedly characterized Russia as an unreliable supplier 

that  weaponizes  its  energy.  Biden  and  his  spokespeople  highlighted  energy 

cooperation  with  democracies  as  part  of  a  broader  ideological  struggle.  In  meetings 

with  world  leaders  the  U.S.  often  emphasized  that  fighting  climate  change  and 

upholding democracy were twin priorities. For instance, in February 2023 Biden told 

Brazil’s  President  Lula  that  the  two  countries  were  united  by  the  need  to  maintain 

democracy,  reach  Russia-Ukraine  peace  while  also tackling the climate crisis195. The 

implication  was  clear:  U.S.  energy  policy  served  not  only  market  goals  but  also  a 

values‑based agenda. In sum, U.S. rhetoric blended traditional security language with 

climate  and  democracy  narratives.  The  administration  labeled  Russian  energy 

‘weaponization’  as  illegitimate  and  depicted  alternatives  (U.S.  gas,  renewables 

finance).  This  messaging  was  intended  to  rally  partner  countries  around  U.S. 

objectives:  by  framing  energy  supply  as  a  matter  of  collective  security  and  shared 

values,  American  leaders  sought  to  keep  allies  aligned  even  as  they  pushed  for 

ambitious emissions reductions.

3.5 Conclusion: The U.S. Influence on Global Energy Markets  

92 

Now  it  is  important  to  assess  the  influence  of  various  U.S.  foreign  policy 

instruments on the transformation of global energy markets. These instruments can be 

broadly  categorized  into  sanctions,  trade  agreements,  diplomacy,  military power and 

climate  policies.  Based  on  previously  conducted  analysis  it  is  possible  to  categorize 

the  actions  into  categories  and  to  assign  weights  to  each  instrument  based  on  its 

relative  influence  on  energy  dynamics;  these  weights  with  examples  are  reflected in 

Table  1.  The  visualization  of  the  impact  of  these  instruments  is  present  in  a  radar 

chart, figure 1, which allows to clearly see which tools have been more significant.  

●  Sanctions (2.5): Sanctions have been a significant tool in reshaping global 

energy markets, particularly by restricting access to key energy players like 

Russia, Iran, and Venezuela — it provoked redirection of global energy flows, 

created opportunities for alternative suppliers, and reduced the dominance of 

certain energy-producing nations. 

●  Trade (2): Trade agreements, particularly LNG exports, have positioned the 

U.S. as a major energy exporter, transforming global energy flows and creating 

opportunities for energy diversification. However, trade is often broader in 

scope and involves longer-term strategies, which is why it is moderately 

influential in transforming energy markets. 

●  Diplomacy (2.5): Energy diplomacy has been central to the U.S.’s efforts to 

diversify global energy sources and ensure long-term energy security. 

Diplomatic initiatives like the U.S.-EU Energy Task Force have been crucial in 

promoting energy diversification, alternative sources of energy, and 

international cooperation on energy security, making it a highly impactful tool 

in the transformation of global energy markets. 

●  Military Power (1): While military power has historically been used to secure 

energy supply routes and maintain stability in key energy-producing regions, its 

 
93 
role in shaping global energy markets has been reduced in recent years. The 

global energy landscape has shifted more toward economic and diplomatic 

tools, and military power now plays a secondary role in influencing energy 

market dynamics, while its past influence is still present in U.S. presence in the 

Middle East. Even the fact of the U.S. financial and intelligence involvement in 

the Ukraine crisis supports its dominant role in EU markets, which is also an 

indirect military effect. 

●  Climate Policies (1.5): Climate policies play a significant role in shaping the 

global energy transition, encouraging the adoption of renewable energy and 

reducing reliance on fossil fuels. Through promotion of renewable energy 

technologies, as well as unconventional energy, the U.S. has indirectly 

influenced global energy markets, driving long-term shifts in energy production 

and consumption.

CHAPTER 4: ANALYSIS OF IMPLICATIONS OF TRANSFORMED 
ENERGY MARKETS FOR RUSSIA AND RUSSIA-EU RELATIONS  

4.1 The Transformation of Russia-EU Energy Relations 

As  mentioned  in  previous  chapters,  the  EU  and  Russia  have  had  strong 

economic ties, with mutual desire to economically integrate since the 1990s. Crises of 

2006  and  2009  (with  Russia  cutting  off  gas  supplies),  2008  (with  concerns  over 

Georgia),  and  Ukraine  conflicts  of  2014  and  2022  were  significant  events  in 

decreasing  trust  between  EU  and  Russia.  Crises  of  2006  and  2009,  despite  raising 

critical  concerns,  did  not  provoke  significant  reduction  of  cooperation  and 

securitization  of  Russia.  But  the  2014  Crimean  crisis  was  followed  by  open 

accusations and real decisions on reduction of Russian supplies as dangerous ones that 

could be used as a weapon. The EU launched an energy security strategy to diversify 

supplies  (investing  in  LNG  terminals,  new  pipelines,  and  renewable  gas;  promoting 

new  trade  partnerships;  plans  on  decreasing  fossil  fuels  dependence).  However, 

pipelines from Russia continued to supply more than 40% of EU gas needs into 2021 

(Council  of  the  European  Union,  2025).  Despite  accusations,  real  reductions  of 

Russian  supplies  began  after  the  2022  crisis,  supported  by  initiatives  such  as  the 

REPowerEU  plan  and  increased  supplies from the USA and Norway (ibid.): in 2024 

overall Russian gas (pipeline and LNG) accounted for only around 19% of EU supply 

(compared  with  33%  from  Norway  and  16.5%  from  the  U.S.).  In  sum,  there  was  a 

shift  from  ‘pre‑2014 dependence’ through a modest ‘diversification’ stage, to a rapid 

‘post‑2022  decoupling’  driven  by  Russia-Ukraine  instability.  The  U.S.  actively 

lobbied  the  EU  to  reduce  Russian  imports  and  supported  increasing  securitizing 

discourse at times it appeared in the EU — it coincided with the USA’s opportunity to 

promote its supplies of LNG (e.g. Trump’s accusations of Nord Stream being a tool of 

coercion  that  undermines  European  security).  After  2022  the  Biden  administration 

and  EU  formed a “Task Force on Energy Security” and U.S. LNG exports to Europe 

 
97 
surged  (from  ~22 bcm  in  2021  to  ~56 bcm  in  2022)196.  U.S.  LNG  thus  became  the 

largest source of imported LNG: in 2023 roughly 48% of EU LNG imports came from 

the  U.S  (EIA,  2024).  At  the  same  time,  the  U.S.  used  hard  power  (sanctions  on 

Russian  pipeline  projects)  and  soft  diplomacy  (energy  guarantees  and  joint 

declarations)  to  encourage  EU  diversification.  Price  comparisons are also important: 

U.S.  LNG  is typically priced at Henry Hub plus liquefaction / shipment costs, which 

historically  has  been  higher  than Russia’s oil‑indexed pipeline price. Russia’s energy 

minister  noted  in  2018  that  the  U.S.  LNG  costs  “30–40%  higher”  than  Russian 

pipeline  gas.197  In  fact,  after  2022  shifts,  Russian  LNG  supplies  to  the  EU  did  not 

diminish,  but  increased. Moreover, their price increased sharply with the EU in 2024 

paying 274% more for Russian LNG, coming mostly from the Arctic, than in 2021198. 

These  actions  may  seem  economically  ‘irrational’,  but  they  show  a  shift  from 

prioritising  interdependence  and  economically  beneficial  trade  to  prioritization  of 

political  motives.  The  EU  is  ready  to  sacrifice  economic  gains  and  pay  more  for 

Norwegian,  American  and  even  the  same  Russian  gas  (that  still  comes  but  not  via 

previous  pipeline  routes).  These  modern  developments  support  constructivist 

arguments  —  securitization  drives actions even if they are ‘irrational’ from the point 

of view of classical IR theories.  

Moreover, the hybrid warfare that began in the U.S.-Russia relations in 2022 is 

important.  It  includes  disinformation  campaigns,  economic  sanctions,  especially 

secondary  sanctions  from  the  USA  —  to  make  other  states  fear  of  being friendly to 

Russia.  The  United  States  is  also  the  founding  and  leading  state  of  NATO,  an 

organisation  created  against  Soviet  communist  threat and that continues to work and 

expand, being a major point of confrontation between Russia and Western forces, with

23 out of 27 European Union states being members of both organisations. All of these 

developments in U.S. foreign policy tools have impacted the European Union.  

Deep  economic  ties  allow  the  U.S.  to  leverage  it  in relations with the EU and 

promote  influence.  The  Transatlantic  Economy  2023  report199  underscores  the 

profound  and  enduring  economic  interdependence  between  the  United  States  and 

Europe,  characterizing  it  as  the  most  deeply  integrated  commercial  partnership 

globally.  Despite  global  shocks  (from  COVID-19  pandemic  and  Russia’s  special 

military  operation  in  Ukraine  to  inflationary  pressures  and  energy  disruptions),  the 

$7.1  trillion  transatlantic  economy  has  shown  remarkable  resilience.  It  remains  the 

largest and wealthiest market in the world, accounting for half of total global personal 

consumption and employing 16 million workers in onshored jobs across both regions. 

This interconnectedness of economies continues to serve as a vital foundation for the 

prosperity, competitiveness, and strategic alignment of both sides of the Atlantic. 

In  an  attempt  to  portray  Russia  as  an  untrustworthy  partner  and  defend  the 

move to alternative energy sources, the U.S. supported the EU in anti-Russian rhetoric 

and  helped  to  enact  policies  that  not  only  guarantee  energy  security  but  also 

complement  larger  Western  strategic  objectives.  Furthermore,  the  EU’s  energy 

alliances  needed  to  be  reevaluated  in  light  of  its  commitment  to  green  energy.   By 

supporting  laws  and  technology  that  encourage  renewable  energy,  the  United  States 

has  established  itself  as  a  major  actor  in  the  energy  transition.  Nowadays  there  are 

continuing  dual  challenges  of  energy  security  and  climate  change  that  have  to  be 

carefully dealt with by the EU. 

In  short,  the  U.S.  policy  was  a  significant  factor,  but  not  the  only  one,  in 

Europe’s  energy  shift.  EU  member  states  also  followed  their  own  security  calculus: 

199  Hamilton,  D.  S.,  &  Quinlan,  J.  P.  (2023).  The  Transatlantic  Economy  2023:  Annual  Survey  of  Jobs,  Trade  and 
Investment  between  the  United  States  and  Europe.  Foreign  Policy  Institute,  Johns  Hopkins  University  SAIS  / 
Transatlantic Leadership Network. https://www.transatlanticrelations.org/  

 
99 
some firms openly proposed resuming limited Russian imports if peace returns200. The 

EU  elite  is  generally  anti-Russian,  despite  opinions  of  states  that  do  not  support 

‘energy  divorce’  with  Russia,  most  officials  stand  for it. For instance, Josep Borrell, 

the  High  Representative  of  the  Union  for  Foreign  Affairs  and  Security  Policy  and 

Vice-President of the European Commission, has a very anti-Russian position, and he 

is  co-chairing  the  U.S.-EU  Energy  Council  with  the  U.S.  Secretary  of  State  and 

relevant  EU  energy  officials,  so  his  opinion  and  views  are  linked  to  the  overall  EU 

direction  regarding  relations  to  Russia  and  energy  supplies.  When  the  EU  put 

sanctions  on  the  Russian  energy  sector  in  March  2022,  Borrell  proclaimed  that  the 

aim was to “make it impossible for Russia to upgrade its oil refineries”201 (Council of 

the  European  Union,  2022).  He  also  said  that  Russia  is  “an  economic  dwarf  —  it’s 

like  a  gas station whose owner has an atomic bomb” (Pérez and Doncel, 2023). This 

discourse  is present in various EU documents and official statements. U.S. narratives 

and propaganda helped to securitize EU perceptions of Russia, but this influence was 

not  the only and primary cause of EU policy changes. Internal concerns are stronger. 

The  EU  has  its  own  strong  anti-Russian position with securitizing discourse steadily 

increasing  since  2006,  but  reaching  its  peak  followed  by  real  actions  in  2022. 

Thoughtful  diversification  and  partnership  strategies  on  all  sides  —  combined  with 

realistic  pricing and recognition of climate trends — will shape the long‑term energy 

and geopolitical landscape.

4.2 Geopolitical and Economic Consequences: Russia’s Energy Pivot to Asia 

Russia’s  economy remains heavily reliant on oil and gas revenues. Fossil fuels 

accounted  for  about  16.5%  of  GDP  in  2023  (1.5%  less  than  in  2022)202.  Western 

sanctions  since  2014  have  targeted  this  sector.  Sanctions  on  LNG  projects  forced 

Novatek to declare force majeure on its Arctic LNG-2 output203, delaying production. 

The  collapse  of  exports  to  Europe  also  hurt  Russia.  In  2023  Europe  accounted  for 

only ~5%204 of Russian crude exports (down from ~40% pre‑2022) and even less of its 

gas.  The  Nord  Stream  sabotage  and  EU  embargoes  meant  Russian  pipeline  gas 

revenue evaporated. In response, Russia has pivoted eastwards — the so-called ‘Pivot 

to  Asia’.  The  Power  of  Siberia  pipeline  to  China  (started  in 2019, eventual capacity 

~38 bcm)205  carries  significant  volumes  and  more  projects  are  planned  (Power  of 

Siberia  2  to  Mongolia  /  China).  Major  LNG  capacity  is  also  being  directed  to Asia. 

The Arctic LNG-2 project (led by Novatek) is designed for 19.8 millions of tonnes per 

year (Mt/year)206 and has secured long‑term deals with Chinese and Indian buyers. For 

example,  Novatek  signed  contracts  to  supply  China’s  ENN  Gas  (~0.6 Mt/year)207  in 

2022  and  Zhejiang  Energy (~1.0 Mt/yr)208 in 2021, and an MoU with India’s Deepak 

Fertilisers209 (volume undisclosed). In oil, Russia has re‑allocated tankers to China and 

India:  by  January  2024,  Russia  was  redirecting  much  of  its  crude  formerly  sold  to 

Europe  to  Asian  markets.  However,  this  pivot  faces  structural  obstacles.  Asian 

markets  are  farther  and  already  served  by  Qatar  /  Australia  LNG,  so  Russia  must

compete on price and reliability. The USA is a competitor on the Asian market which 

already  threatens  Australian  dominance  on  this  market  — which  is  main  LNG 

supplier of Japan and South Korea. In 2022, Japan was the largest buyer of Australian 

LNG,  accounting  for  about  40%  of total sales, followed by China, South Korea, and 

Taiwan210.  Meg  O’Neill,  chief  executive  of  Woodside  Energy (Australian company), 

claimed  that  Australia  “loses on every front”211 to the US — the reason is high taxes 

and costs of labour.  

However,  the  financial  part  is  important  here:  Chinese  contracts  reportedly 

come  with  steep  discounts,  squeezing  Russian margins. Russia forecasted that China 

will  pay  much  lower  prices:  about  $271–297  per  1,000 cubic  meters  vs  ~$480  per 

1,000 cubic  meters  charged  to  Europe  in  2023212.  Infrastructure  remains  limited:  no 

major  pipeline  reaches  India,  and  Russia’s  pipelines  to  China  cannot  be  built 

overnight.  Domestically,  Russia  faces  a  shrinking  Euro‑demand  for  fuel while being 

increasingly dependent on a few Asian customers. In sum, while the Eastward pivot is 

real, it entails reinvestment and exposes Russia to new vulnerabilities.

4.3 Future Implications and Policy Recommendations 

102 

Recommendations for Russia 

Understanding  U.S.  foreign  policy  instruments  is  crucial  in  navigating  the 

evolving  global  energy  landscape.  Knowing  that  the  USA  primarily  acts  through 

sanctions  and  diplomacy,  Russia  should  recognize  the  importance  of  energy 

diplomacy  and  actively  seek new multilateral agreements to secure long-term energy 

security.  Returning  to  the  European  market  at  pre‑2022  levels  now  seems  unlikely 

without  a  major  political  shift.  European  leaders  have  pledged  to  phase  out Russian 

energy  by  2027213,  and  even  in  the  event  of  peace  normalization  is  not  very  likely 

(perhaps  on  the  order  of  20–30%  of  former  volumes).  Therefore,  Russia  should 

accelerate  economic  diversification  beyond  hydrocarbons.  In  the  near  term  it  can 

deepen  ties  in  Asia:  completing  Power  of  Siberia  2,  expanding  LNG  capacity  (e.g. 

Arctic  LNG  expansion),  and  forging  more  gas‑for-industry  deals  with China / India. 

Simultaneously,  Russia  could  benefit  by  developing  non‑gas  sectors  at  home  (e.g. 

petrochemicals,  renewables)  as  well  as  other  sectors  of  the  economy  in  order  to 

reduce budget dependence, though these initiatives are not new, in practical terms the 

budget  is  still dependent on material resources revenue. Transparency on pricing and 

adherence to contracts will help build trust with new partners, which is already shown 

by Chinese deals. By understanding and adapting to these U.S. strategies, Russia can 

better anticipate challenges and position itself for greater energy security in a shifting 

geopolitical environment. 

End of shale revolution? 

While  the  U.S.  shale  revolution  and  LNG  exports  reshaped  global  energy 

markets,  several  challenges  are  faced  by  the  USA.  Shale  wells  have  high  initial 

production  rates but decline rapidly, requiring continuous drilling to maintain output. 

213  European  Commission.  (2025,  May  6).  EU  to  fully  end  its  dependency  on  Russian  energy  [Press  release]. 
https://ec.europa.eu/commission/presscorner/detail/en/ip_25_1131  

 
103 
The  physical  limits  of  shale  formations  will  eventually  constrain  production, raising 

questions  about  the  long-term  sustainability  of  the  shale  boom.  Moreover,  the  U.S. 

Department  of  Energy  (2024)  highlighted  potential  domestic  implications  of 

increasing  LNG  exports.  Higher  LNG  exports  could  lead  to  increased  domestic 

natural  gas  prices,  which  may  then  affect  electricity  prices and manufacturing costs. 

This  underscores  the  need  for  careful  consideration  of  the  balance  between  export 

growth  and  domestic  energy  affordability:  “DOE  analysis  exposes  a  triple-cost 

increase  to  U.S.  consumers  from  increasing  LNG  exports  –  the  increasing  domestic 

price  of  the  natural  gas  itself,  increases  in  electricity  prices  (natural gas being a key 

input  in  many  U.S.  power  markets),  and  the  increased  costs  for consumers from the 

pass-through of higher costs to U.S. manufacturers.”214. Therefore, the long-term U.S. 

influence on the global LNG market and availability to offer favorable conditions for 

exports  of  high  amounts  of  energy  products  is  questioned.  Overall,  the  shale  boom 

and  the  development  of  U.S.  LNG  exports  totally  reoriented  the  landscape  of world 

energy. By becoming the world's leading producer of oil and natural gas, the U.S. not 

only  increased  its  energy  security  but  also  substantially  increased  its  geopolitical 

influence.  U.S.  LNG  became  a  vital  tool  for  diversifying  world  energy supplies and 

reducing Europe's dependence on Russian gas. But the long-term sustainability of the 

shale  boom  is  not  guaranteed.  The  coming  decade  will  show  whether  the  U.S.  can 

sustain its leadership or if a more multipolar energy regime will emerge. 

The  global  gas  market  is  entering  a  longer  transition.  As  major  economies 

decarbonize,  overall  natural  gas  demand  growth  will  slow  or  even  decline  by 

mid‑century215.  To  keep  the  U.S.  LNG  competitive,  producers  should  mitigate 

environmental  concerns:  invest  in  methane  leak  reduction  and  carbon  capture  at

liquefaction  facilities.  Flexible  contracts  (shorter  duration,  destination  clauses)  will 

make  U.S.  LNG  more  attractive  to  buyers  wary  of  stranded  assets.  U.S.  energy 

diplomacy  must  also  adapt:  emphasize  gas  as  a  bridging  fuel  in  the  clean‑energy 

transition  and  package  LNG  exports  with  cooperation  on  renewables  and  energy 

efficiency. For example, the U.S. could expand incentives (loan guarantees or grants) 

for  countries to modernize their gas infrastructure or build regasification terminals in 

exchange  for  LNG  commitments.  Diplomatically,  the  U.S.  should  work  through 

forums like the U.S.-EU Energy Council to align gas exports with climate goals (e.g. 

by supporting green-hydrogen hubs in Europe that complement LNG use).  

From petrodollar to gas-dollar? 

Existing  U.S.  policy  signals  a  move  to  replicate  the  petrodollar  system  in  the 

LNG sector — so-called "gas-dollar" strategy. By promoting dollar-denominated sales 

contracts  for  LNG  and  constructing  export  facilities,  the  U.S.  aims  to  secure  its 

position  in  global  gas  markets.  Much  like  the  petrodollar  system,  the  gas-dollar 

strategy aims to promote the dominance of U.S. natural gas in global trade and ensure 

that  these  transactions  are  conducted  primarily  in  U.S.  dollars.  This  approach  is  not 

only driven by economic motives but also reflects broader geopolitical objectives. The 

gas-dollar  strategy  is  part  of  a  wider  effort by the U.S. to secure market share in the 

global  LNG  trade  and  reduce  the  influence  of  rival  energy  powers,  particularly 

Russia.  Unlike  traditional  long-term  contracts  used  by  Russia  and  Qatar,  U.S.  LNG 

contracts  often  offer  more  flexibility in pricing and delivery schedules, making them 

attractive  to  buyers  in  Europe  and  Asia.  For  instance,  U.S.  LNG  is  usually  priced 

based  on  the  Henry  Hub  natural  gas  benchmark,  offering  an  alternative  to  the 

oil-linked  pricing  models  commonly  used  by  other  suppliers.  This  pricing  structure 

can be advantageous for buyers seeking to diversify their price exposure (MEI, 2024). 

However,  the  global  energy  landscape  has  changed  significantly  since  the  1970s. 

Rising  nations  like  China  and  India  increasingly  demand  energy  trade  in  home 

 
105 
currencies.  China  has  been  actively  promoting  the  use  of  its  currency,  the  yuan,  in 

international  energy  transactions,  aiming  to  challenge  the  dominance  of  the  U.S. 

dollar  in  the  global  energy  market.  Russia  pursues  a  creation  and  stabilisation  of  a 

multipolar  international  order,  particularly  pursuing  multipolar  energy  policy,  also 

promoting  the  use  of  home  currencies  in  transactions  with  China,  India,  and  other 

allies. In October 2023, the China National Petroleum Corporation (CNPC) completed 

a  transaction  settling  1  million  barrels  of  crude  oil  using  digital  yuan,  marking  a 

significant step in yuan-based energy trade (Global Times, 2023). Moreover, Russia’s 

focus  on  real  assets  —  such  as  physical  infrastructure  and  long-term  supply 

agreements  —  contrasts  with  the  U.S.’s  more  market-oriented  approach.  The  EU's 

ambitious  climate goals aim to phase out fossil fuels, including natural gas, by 2050, 

reducing  the  potential  market  for  the  U.S.  LNG216.  This  transition  is  expected  to 

reduce  the  potential  market for U.S. LNG exports to Europe. Additionally, the EU is 

drafting its first 2040 climate target, aiming for a 90% reduction in net greenhouse gas 

emissions  by  that  date  compared  to  1990  levels.  Recommendations  for  his  plan 

included an 80% reduction in fossil fuel use for energy by 2040217, further diminishing 

the  role  of  natural  gas  in  the  EU's  energy  mix  —  a  challenge  for  Russia  as well, as 

supplies  of  Russian  gas  did  not  stop  and  still  bring  revenue,  either  through  shadow 

fleet, re-sales from India, LNG or TurkStream. For Russia, the “gas-dollar” represents 

both  a  challenge  and  an  opportunity  to  promote  a  more  diversified  and  multipolar 

energy system.

CONCLUSION 

This  study  investigated  the  United  States’  role  in  reshaping  global  energy 

markets  between  2008  and  2024,  with  specific  emphasis  on  its  influence  on  Russia 

and  Russia-EU  energy  relations.  In  exploring  U.S.  foreign  policy  instruments  — 

sanctions,  trade  agreements,  diplomacy,  military  power  and  climate  policies  —  the 

research  found  how  the USA has leveraged its energy resources to drive geopolitical 

and  economic  changes  on  the  global  stage.  The  research  question,  which  sought  to 

assess  how  the  U.S.  influenced  global  energy  markets  and  the  EU-Russia  energy 

relationship,  was  addressed  by  examining  the  evolution  of  U.S.  actions  over several 

critical  periods  between  2008  and  2024.  U.S.  energy  policy  radically  shifted, 

especially  with  the  shale  revolution  that  transformed  the  world  energy  balance  by 

turning the U.S. from an energy importer into a key energy exporter. This shift played 

a  pivotal  role  in  reconfiguring  the energy supply lines, particularly in Europe, where 

the U.S. became a leading LNG exporter in competition with traditional suppliers like 

Russia.  The  U.S.  also  employed  a  range  of  hard  and  soft  power  instruments  to 

undermine Russia’s energy dominance in Europe. This was increasingly evident from 

2014,  when  the  Crimean  crisis  and  subsequent  sanctions  escalated  U.S.-Russia 

tensions,  and  the  EU  openly  engaged  in  discourse  to  reduce  reliance  on  Russian 

resources.  Despite  U.S.  efforts  to  promote  its  LNG  as  an  alternative  to Russian gas, 

the EU’s internal divisions and economic considerations created a complex landscape, 

with some member states pushing for continued cooperation with Russia. Overall, the 

U.S.  influence  on  the  EU was clearly present, but did not represent a deciding force, 

rather, an important partner, whose opinion was closely considered. 

The study also analyzed the broader implications of American energy policy in 

global  markets.  Through  the  analysis  of  instruments,  research  found  that  energy 

diplomacy and sanctions have affected international energy markets to a bigger extent. 

The United States has further employed the exports of energy, particularly LNG, as an 

 
107 
instrument  to  foster  strategic  partnerships,  reinforce  its  geopolitical  influence,  and 

reduce  dependence  of  the  EU  on  Russian  energy.  Military  power,  while  historically 

used  by  the  U.S.,  has  played  a  less  important  role  in  recent  years,  with  the  U.S. 

increasingly  relying  on  economic  and  diplomatic  tools  to  achieve  its  strategic 

objectives.  

Historically,  the  U.S. has used a combination of hard and soft power strategies 

to  secure  its  position  in  global  energy  markets,  adjusting  its  policies  to  match 

geopolitical  changes  and  national  energy  developments.  U.S.  engagement  in  energy 

markets has been particularly pronounced when new geopolitical shifts occur, such as 

post-2022  EU,  or  when  domestic  production  shifts,  like  the shale boom. U.S. policy 

has  evolved  with  each  administration,  reflecting  the  priorities  and  strategies  of  the 

sitting  president,  with  a  shift  from  focusing  on  energy  security  to  promoting  U.S. 

energy  dominance,  particularly  during  strategic  crises.  Before  2008,  U.S.  energy 

policy  was  centered  around  military  power  and  economic  diplomacy  aimed  at 

securing  energy  resources,  especially  in  the  Middle  East.  The  petrodollar  system 

helped  ensure  global  oil  transactions  were  conducted  in  U.S.  dollars,  solidifying 

America’s  power.  These  actions  laid  the  groundwork  for  the  U.S.’s  eventual 

leadership  in  global  energy  markets.  The  policy  shift  toward  unconventional  energy 

production  began  with  the  Energy  Policy  Act  of  2005,  which  prioritized  the 

development of shale gas. With the shale revolution, this led the U.S. to promote LNG 

exports and establish itself as a potential energy supplier to Europe and other markets. 

At  the  same  time,  U.S.  sanctions  on  Iran  and  Venezuela  reinforced  American 

dominance  in global energy, while also encouraging EU energy security cooperation. 

Following  Russia’s  2014  annexation  of  Crimea,  the  U.S.  imposed  sanctions  on 

Russia’s  energy  sector,  reducing  its  influence  over  European  energy  supplies.  U.S. 

LNG  exports  to  the  EU  increased,  capitalizing  on  Europe’s  rising  demand  for 

alternatives  to  Russian  gas, and the U.S.-EU Energy Council was created to advance 

 
108 
energy diversification and market integration. The Russian Special Military Operation 

in Ukraine in 2022 marked a major shift, with the U.S. imposing sanctions on Russian 

energy  and becoming the largest LNG supplier to Europe. The EU-U.S. Energy Task 

Force,  formed  in  2022,  cemented this partnership to ensure Europe’s energy security 

and  reduce  its  dependence  on  Russian  energy.  Additionally,  the  U.S.  continued  to 

promote  itself  as  a  leader  in  the  green  energy  transition. The U.S. capitalized on the 

crisis  to  strengthen  its  leadership  in  the  global  LNG market while also furthering its 

role  in  the  global  energy  transition  and  decarbonization  efforts.  LNG  exports  will 

remain  a  dominant force in the EU, but increased domestic natural gas consumption, 

along  with  natural  limits  of  U.S.  shale  production,  will  likely  constrain  export 

capacity.  As  a  result,  the  global  energy  market  is  becoming  increasingly  multipolar, 

with regional powers like Russia, China, and India strengthening their influence. This 

shift opens opportunities for Russia to diversify its production, trade, and alliances. 

In conclusion, the hypothesis that the U.S. utilized both hard and soft power to 

facilitate the EU shift away from Russian energy supplies and that the U.S. benefited 

most  from  the  reorganization  of  global  energy  markets  was  broadly  confirmed.  The 

U.S.  influence,  while  immense,  was  not  purely  deterministic,  and  the  EU’s  internal 

policy  and  interests  and  broader  geopolitical  forces  decided  the  final  outcome  of  its 

energy  policy.  The  shift  in  energy  relations  has  made  the  U.S.  a  key  player  in 

international  energy  markets,  but  with  challenges  like  the  rise  of  new  players  like 

China  and  India,  and  the  enhanced  EU  emphasis  on  decarbonization.  This  research 

has confirmed that U.S. energy policies have played a transformative role in reshaping 

global energy flows, the EU’s energy strategy, and its relations with Russia.
